Great, B200 with SM100. We have triton 3.6 and CUDA 13.2. Let me design my approach. The problem is top-k over the last dim of a 2D fp32 tensor. Shapes: - (1, 131072, 64) - decoder vocab top-k - (64, 8192, 8) - prefill / attention top-k - (32, 16384, 32) - mid-size batched - (16, 12000, 16) - non-power-of-2 n stress - (128, 4096, 1) - batched argmax Total bytes: For (1, 131072, 64) - input 524KB. For (64, 8192, 8) - input 2MB. Top-k is bandwidth-bound. For batch=1, n=131072: This is a single row. The output is 64 floats + 64 int64 = 768 bytes. Input is 524288 bytes. Ratio is 1:682. Pure bandwidth-bound - just need to read input efficiently. For batch=128, n=4096, k=1: 128 rows, need argmax of 4096 floats per row. Strategy: I'll write a Triton kernel that processes one row per program (or multiple). For each row, we can: 1. Read the full row into a single block (if it fits in shared memory) and do a sort. 2. Use a radix-select / tree reduction approach for large n. Actually, for top-k with moderate k, the best approach is: - Each program processes one row (or several rows for small n) - Load the row into registers/SMEM - Use a single-pass approach: - For k=1: just reduce (max + argmax) - For small k: use a small priority queue / tournament sort - For larger k: use a partial bitonic sort, or repeated argmax with masking Let me think more carefully: For (1, 131072, 64): - One row of 131072 floats - Triton block can have up to BLOCK_SIZE=131072 to load it all at once - Then we need to find the top 64 For (128, 4096, 1): argmax, easy For (16, 12000, 16): non-power-of-2 n, so BLOCK_SIZE=16384 (next power of 2) with mask For small n (4096-16384), we can load the full row in one block. For (1, 131072), we may need to chunk. Approach 1: Single-pass top-k using bitonic sort within block - BLOCK_SIZE = next power of 2 of n - Load row with mask - Run bitonic sort - Return first k values - For k=64, n=131072: this would be 131072 elements per block, but we need top-64. We can sort descending and take first 64. But the bitonic sort is O(n log²n) - 131072 * 17² ≈ 38M compares. That's a lot of compute per block. - Better: use radix-style approach or just first find the threshold then filter. Approach 2: Tournament / heap-based top-k - Maintain a small buffer of top-k candidates - Iterate through row, updating buffer - This is O(n * k) but if k is small, it works well Approach 3: Multi-pass: - Pass 1: find approximate threshold (e.g., 99th percentile) - Pass 2: filter elements above threshold, then sort exactly - This requires two passes through data Let me think about what's actually fastest on a B200: - 8 TB/s HBM3e bandwidth - For (1, 131072, 64): 524288 bytes / 8 TB/s ≈ 65 ns minimum read time Actually, let me think about the bitonic sort approach more carefully. The issue with n=131072 is that the bitonic sort is O(n log²n / 2) = 131072 * 17²/2 ≈ 19M compare-exchange operations. Each takes a few cycles. On a B200 SM at 2 GHz, that's a lot. Let me estimate: 19M ops at 2 GHz = 10 ms? No, way more parallel. Actually let me think about an entirely different approach. For top-k of a single row: - If n is large and k is small: use tournament sort or radix select - If n is small: just bitonic sort For our cases: - (1, 131072, 64): n/k = 2048 - (64, 8192, 8): n/k = 1024 - (32, 16384, 32): n/k = 512 - (16, 12000, 16): n/k = 750 - (128, 4096, 1): n/k = inf (argmax) One solid approach: For each row, use a single block (CTA) and: 1. Load the row into shared memory 2. Run a small "k-pass" sort: maintain a sorted buffer of top-k 3. For each element, binary search into the buffer, evict if smaller Or: Just sort the entire thing with bitonic and take top-k. For n=131072 that's actually still tractable if we do it efficiently. Actually, let me think again. For the (1, 131072, 64) case, we only have 1 row. We need to fully utilize the GPU. So the algorithm should be: - Each CTA processes part of the row, gets a local top-k - Then reduce across CTAs to get global top-k But with one row, this becomes a single-row reduction. We could use multiple CTAs in parallel and do a final reduction. Hmm, let me think about a different strategy: A single Triton program processes the entire row in chunks. Use registers to maintain a top-k tournament tree. This avoids the need for inter-CTA reduction. Let me design a concrete approach for each shape: For (1, 131072, 64): - Use 1 CTA. Load in chunks of 4096 or 8192, maintain top-64 buffer. - After all chunks, sort the top-64 buffer and output. For (64, 8192, 8): - 64 CTAs, one per row. Each CTA loads 8192 elements, maintains top-8. For (32, 16384, 32): - 32 CTAs, one per row. Each CTA loads 16384 elements, maintains top-32. For (16, 12000, 16): - 16 CTAs, one per row. Each CTA loads 16384 elements (with mask for 12000), maintains top-16. For (128, 4096, 1): - 128 CTAs, one per row. Each CTA does argmax. This is the "one row per CTA" approach. Let me think about how to implement the top-k per CTA. The simplest approach is the "k-pass" approach: - Maintain a sorted top-k buffer in registers - For each new element, do a linear or binary scan to find where it goes - If the element is bigger than the smallest in the top-k, insert it (and shift others) For k=64, this means 64 comparisons per element. For n=131072, that's 8M comparisons. At 2 GHz that's still a lot but the parallelism helps. Alternative: Use Triton's tl.sort to sort the full row, then take the top k. But the full sort with 131072 elements... Triton has some limits. Actually, the best approach for n large, k small: use a "merge top-k" approach: - Each warp/block loads a chunk, finds local top-k via bitonic - Then merge OR for very large n: use a radix-select approach. Let me start with a simpler, more pragmatic approach: Approach A: One CTA per row, bitonic sort the full row, take top-k. - Pros: Simple, works for all k - Cons: For n=131072, the bitonic sort is expensive Approach B: One CTA per row, load full row in registers, use a tournament / heap sort. - Pros: O(n log k) work - Cons: More complex Approach C: One CTA per row, iterative top-k (k passes of argmax with masking). - Pros: Simple - Cons: O(n*k) memory reads Let me check if Triton can handle a block of 131072 elements. The Triton block size limit is 65536 by default I think but can be larger with reconfiguration. Let me check. Actually let me think about the GPU resource constraints. A B200 SM has 128 KB shared memory and 64 KB registers. 131072 floats = 512KB, way more than fits in shared memory or registers. So we can't load the whole row at once. So for n=131072, we need to: 1. Chunk the data and process in pieces 2. Use a "running top-k" approach 3. OR use multiple CTAs per row and reduce For multi-CTA per row: For (1, 131072, 64), we could use 4 CTAs each handling 32768 elements. Each finds its local top-64. Then a final reduction merges 4 * 64 = 256 elements down to 64. This is much faster than 1 CTA. Hmm, but for n=8192 or smaller, one CTA per row is fine since it fits in shared memory. Let me go with the following approach: - For each row, use multiple CTAs (or one) to find local top-k - For multi-CTA, reduce But actually, let me think about this differently. The simplest Triton approach is: ```python @triton.jit def topk_kernel(x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, n, k, BLOCK_N): # Each program processes one row, in chunks row = tl.program_id(0) # Initialize top-k buffer top_vals = tl.full((BLOCK_K,), -inf, tl.float32) top_idxs = tl.full((BLOCK_K,), -1, tl.int32) for chunk_start in range(0, n, BLOCK_N): offs = chunk_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < n vals = tl.load(x_ptr + row * n + offs, mask=mask, other=-inf) # merge vals into top_vals ... # Sort top_vals descending ... # Store tl.store(val_ptr + row * k + ..., top_vals) tl.store(idx_ptr + row * k + ..., top_idxs) ``` The tricky part is "merge vals into top_vals". Approach 1: Sort concatenated (top_vals + vals) and take top k. But this means sorting BLOCK_N + BLOCK_K elements every chunk. Approach 2: For each element in vals, if it's > min(top_vals), insert it. This is O(BLOCK_N * k) per chunk. Approach 3: Bitonic merge. The top-k buffer is sorted, vals is unsorted. Sort vals, then bitonic merge top_vals and sorted_vals, take top k. Approach 4: Tournament/heap. Maintain a min-heap of top-k. For each element, if > heap min, replace and reheapify. For k=64, BLOCK_N=4096: - Approach 1: sort 4160 elements per chunk. 33 chunks for n=131072. So 33 sorts of 4160 elements. - Approach 2: 4096*64 = 262144 comparisons per chunk. 33 chunks = 8.6M comparisons. Too many. - Approach 3: 33 chunks * (sort 4096 + bitonic merge 4160). Bitonic merge is O((p+q) log²(p+q)). - Approach 4: similar to 2 but with heap operations. Actually, the simplest efficient approach is: 1. Sort the full row in chunks using bitonic sort within a block 2. Then take top-k from the sorted result But for n=131072, we can't sort 131072 elements in a single block. Let me think about it differently. What if we do this: - Each program processes one row - Load the row in chunks of, say, 4096 or 8192 - Maintain a sorted top-K buffer (size = k) in registers - For each chunk: merge chunk's elements with top-k For the merge, we can do: - Sort the chunk (bitonic, BLOCK_N elements) - Then bitonic-merge sorted chunk with sorted top-k - Take the top k from the merge Bitonic merge of two sorted arrays of size p and q: O((p+q) * log(p+q)) For BLOCK_N=4096, k=64: each chunk merge is ~5000 * log(5000) ≈ 60K compare-exchanges. 33 chunks for n=131072: 33 * 60K = 2M compare-exchanges. Let me think about the implementation: ```python @triton.jit def topk_kernel(x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, B, N, K, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr): # Each program handles one row pid = tl.program_id(0) # Maintain top-k as a sorted (descending) array in registers # Use negative infinity for "empty" slots NEG_INF = float('-inf') top_v = tl.full((BLOCK_K,), NEG_INF, tl.float32) top_i = tl.full((BLOCK_K,), -1, tl.int32) # Process in chunks for chunk_start in range(0, N, BLOCK_N): offs = chunk_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N x_offs = pid * N + offs v = tl.load(x_ptr + x_offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) i = offs.to(tl.int32) # Sort v descending (triton sort) v_sorted, i_sorted = sort(v, i) # descending sort # Now bitonic-merge with top_v # We want the top k of (v_sorted, top_v) where both are sorted desc # ... ``` Hmm, the bitonic merge in Triton is a bit complex. Actually, here's a cleaner approach: ```python @triton.jit def topk_kernel(x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, N, K, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr): pid = tl.program_id(0) base = pid * N # Load the full row offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=-float('inf')) # Need original indices i = offs.to(tl.int32) # For k iterations: for k_ in range(K): # Find argmax of v max_idx = tl.argmax(v, axis=0) max_val = tl.max(v) # Store tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k_, max_val) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k_, i[max_idx]) # Mask out the selected element v = tl.where(tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) == max_idx, -float('inf'), v) ``` This is K passes through the row, each doing a full reduction. For (1, 131072, 64): 64 passes * (load 524KB + argmax over 131072). For the argmax, the cost is O(BLOCK_N) per pass, which is fast. The load is K * BLOCK_N = 64 * 131072 = 8M elements per row. Then 17 levels of reduction = 17 * 8 = 136 cycles per reduction. 64 reductions * 136 = 8700 cycles. At 2 GHz, that's 4.4 μs. So the work is ~5 μs, dominated by reduction tree. Then 6 levels of warp-internal reduction (log2(32)) = 6 cycles. Then 6 levels of inter-warp reduction (log2(64)) = 6 * 1 = 6 cycles. Total per reduction: 64 * 1 + 12 = 76 cycles? Let me code it up: ```python import torch import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def topk_kernel( x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, N, K, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, K_CONST: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base = pid * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') # Load the full row offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) i = offs.to(tl.int32) for k in tl.static_range(K_CONST): # Argmax max_idx = tl.argmax(v, axis=0) max_val = tl.max(v) # Store tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K_CONST + k, max_val) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K_CONST + k, i[max_idx]) # Mask out v = tl.where(offs == max_idx, NEG_INF, v) ``` Wait, there's an issue: `tl.argmax` returns a scalar index, but `tl.where(offs == max_idx, ...)` works on a tensor. For each row, find the kth largest value (using radix select or sort) 2. Here's my plan: **Solution 1: Per-row, chunked, k-iteration** ```python import torch import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def topk_kernel( x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, N, K, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, # chunk size, power of 2, >= K K_CONST: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base = pid * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') # Process in chunks for chunk_start in range(0, N, BLOCK_N): offs = chunk_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) # K iterations to find top K of this chunk for k in tl.static_range(K_CONST): # Argmax over the chunk max_idx = tl.argmax(v, axis=0) max_val = tl.max(v) real_idx = chunk_start + max_idx # Store # ... # Mask out local_offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) v = tl.where(local_offs == max_idx, NEG_INF, v) ``` The issue: storing per-iteration results. We need to write to a buffer at index k, but Triton doesn't support that. We CAN store to a 2D buffer: ```python # Pre-allocate 2D buffer for outputs val_buf = tl.zeros((K_CONST, BLOCK_N), tl.float32) idx_buf = tl.zeros((K_CONST, BLOCK_N), tl.int32) # In each iteration k: # ... we can update val_buf[k, max_idx] = max_val? But this requires dynamic indexing ``` Actually, we can't dynamically index a tensor in Triton. But we can use `tl.where` to set values: ```python # For iteration k: val_buf = val_buf + tl.where(local_offs == max_idx, max_val, 0) # ugh, only works if max_idx is unique ``` Hmm, this only works if each max_idx is unique. They will be since we mask out after each iteration. OK so: ```python val_buf = tl.zeros((K_CONST, BLOCK_N), tl.float32) idx_buf = tl.zeros((K_CONST, BLOCK_N), tl.int32) for k in tl.static_range(K_CONST): max_idx = tl.argmax(v, axis=0) max_val = tl.max(v) real_idx = chunk_start + max_idx # Update row k of buffers val_buf_row_k = tl.where(local_offs == max_idx, max_val, 0.0) # vector of size BLOCK_N idx_buf_row_k = tl.where(local_offs == max_idx, real_idx, 0) # Now set val_buf[k, :] = val_buf_row_k # We can do this with: val_buf = val_buf + something, but that adds to all rows # Or: create a (K_CONST, BLOCK_N) mask and use it ``` Hmm, the issue is updating a specific row. We can use a mask: ```python # We want: val_buf[k, :] = val_buf_row_k for the kth iteration # Approach: pre-build a mask of which row we're writing to # For each iteration k, the mask is: (tl.arange(0, K_CONST)[:, None] == k) # Then val_buf = tl.where(mask_2d, broadcast(val_buf_row_k), val_buf) # But this requires broadcasting and dynamic masks ``` Actually I think the cleanest way is: - Allocate val_buf as (K_CONST, BLOCK_N) - In each iteration k, broadcast val_buf_row_k to fill the kth row ```python val_buf = tl.zeros((K_CONST, BLOCK_N), tl.float32) # ... for k in tl.static_range(K_CONST): val_buf_row_k = tl.where(local_offs == max_idx, max_val, 0.0) # shape (BLOCK_N,) # Broadcast to (K_CONST, BLOCK_N) val_buf_row_k_broadcast = tl.broadcast_to(val_buf_row_k[None, :], (K_CONST, BLOCK_N)) # Build mask k_arr = tl.arange(0, K_CONST)[:, None] row_mask = k_arr == k # shape (K_CONST, 1) # Apply val_buf = tl.where(row_mask, val_buf_row_k_broadcast, val_buf) ``` Hmm, this is getting complex. And we'd then need to compact the result. Let me try a different approach: just do the argmax + mask K times within the chunk, then write the K results to a local sorted array, then merge with running top-k. Actually, let me try a much simpler approach: store the argmax index as a scalar, but use shared memory to collect the K results. ```python # In each iteration: # - max_val is a scalar # - max_idx is a scalar # We need to write max_val to a memory location, but we can't have a "running buffer" in registers ``` Triton doesn't have "mutable registers" like that. So we need to use shared memory or global memory. OK, here's another approach: process the entire row at once if it fits, or do it in two phases. From Triton docs/source: the max block size is typically determined by `num_warps` and per-thread resources. With 8 warps (256 threads) and 32 elements per thread, you get 8192-element blocks. With 16 warps and 64 elements per thread, you get 16384. For (1, 131072, 64), we need to process 131072 elements. Let me write a first version. ```python """TopK kernel via iterative argmax over chunks.""" import torch import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def topk_kernel( x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, N: tl.constexpr, # Or runtime? K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base = pid * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') # Load full row in chunks and find top-K iteratively # We need to do K iterations of argmax over the full row # Since we can't store the full row, we chunk # Maintain a "mask" of which elements are still in play # For each chunk: load it, do K iterations, but combine with previous # Simplest: process the row in one go if it fits, else do partial # For now, assume N <= BLOCK_N (fits in one block) offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) # K iterations for k in tl.static_range(K): max_idx = tl.argmax(v, axis=0) max_val = tl.max(v) real_idx = max_idx # since chunk_start = 0 # Store tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k, max_val) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k, real_idx) # Mask out v = tl.where(offs == max_idx, NEG_INF, v) ``` For this to work, BLOCK_N >= N. For N=131072, BLOCK_N must be 131072. That's 131072 floats per CTA = 512KB. Won't fit. So we need chunking. Let me design that. Plan: For each row, use one CTA. Chunk the data into BLOCK_N-sized pieces. Maintain a running top-K buffer (BLOCK_K elements). For each chunk, find local top-K, then merge. But the issue is: when we find local top-K via K iterations, we accumulate K values. We need to store them in a buffer to merge with the next chunk. Since we can't easily store a K-element vector per chunk (would need shared memory or a 2D buffer), let's do this: After loading each chunk, do K iterations of argmax. For each iteration, write the result to a (K, BLOCK_N) buffer. At the end, "extract" the K non-zero entries from the buffer and merge. Or simpler: maintain the top-K as a sorted list. Wait, here's a different idea. We can have the CTA process the entire row by: 1. Loading chunk 1, doing K iterations, storing K results 2. Final sort: 2048 * log²(2048) / 2 ≈ 2048 * 66 = 135K ops. Easy. So: - Each chunk: 64 iterations of argmax (over 4096 elements) = 64 * 4096 = 262K ops - 32 chunks: 8.4M ops - Final sort of 2048 elements + take top 64: 135K ops - Total: 8.5M ops For 8 TB/s memory: - Load 131072 floats: 524KB / 8 TB/s = 65ns - We do 32 chunk loads, so 32 * 65ns = 2μs (but with L2 caching, this could be just 1 * 65ns = 65ns) So memory time is 65ns-2μs. Compute time is ~5μs (estimate). So compute-bound, not memory-bound. Hmm. The k-iteration approach has a lot of compute. Let me think about a more efficient approach: bitonic top-k. Bitonic top-k: Run a bitonic sort, but only the top K need to be in the right order. The rest can be anything. This is a "partial bitonic sort" that can be faster than full sort. Actually, the simplest efficient approach: full bitonic sort, take top K. The compute is O(N log²N), but with parallelism it's fast. For (1, 131072, 64) full bitonic sort: 131072 * 17² / 2 ≈ 19M compares. At 2 GHz with 32 elements/cycle/SM, this is 19M / 32 / 2e9 = 0.3 ms = 300μs. Way too slow. Wait, that's not right. Bitonic sort has log²(N)/2 compare-exchange layers, each with N/2 compare-exchanges. For N=131072: 17²/2 = 144 layers, each with 65536 compare-exchanges. Total: 144 * 65536 ≈ 9.4M compare-exchanges. Each compare-exchange is ~2 instructions (compare + conditional swap). So 19M instructions. On a single SM at 2 GHz: 19M / (128 cores * 2e9) = 75ns. Wait, that's fast. Actually 128 cores * 2 GHz = 256G ops/sec. 19M ops / 256G = 74ns. So full bitonic sort of 131072 elements on one SM should take ~100ns. Hmm, that's a lot faster than I thought. The split operation works on the last dim, but if we flatten back and split again, it works. Let me just try the simplest thing: split v_sorted log2(BLOCK_N/K) times to get top (BLOCK_N / 2^log2(BLOCK_N/K)) = K elements. ```python NUM_SPLITS: tl.constexpr = ... # log2(BLOCK_N // K) v_cur = v_sorted i_cur = i_sorted for _ in tl.static_range(NUM_SPLITS): v_first, _ = tl.split(v_cur) i_first, _ = tl.split(i_cur) v_cur = v_first i_cur = i_first # v_cur now has K elements ``` OK let me just code this up and test. Actually I realize one issue: tl.split signature in Triton 3.6. Let me check. From Triton 3.6 docs: `tl.split(x)` - splits a tensor in half along the last dim. Returns two tensors. The input must have even size on the last dim. So `tl.split(v_sorted)` where v_sorted is (BLOCK_N,) returns two (BLOCK_N // 2,) tensors. Great. Let me code: ```python @triton.jit def topk_kernel( x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, N: tl.constexpr, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, NUM_SPLITS: tl.constexpr, # log2(BLOCK_N // K) ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base = pid * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) i = offs.to(tl.int32) v_sorted, i_sorted = tl.sort(v, i, dim=0, descending=True) # Extract top K via repeated split v_cur = v_sorted i_cur = i_sorted for _ in tl.static_range(NUM_SPLITS): v_first, _ = tl.split(v_cur) i_first, _ = tl.split(i_cur) v_cur = v_first i_cur = i_first # v_cur has K elements (the top K) k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, v_cur) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, i_cur) ``` Now I need to handle the case where BLOCK_N is too large (e.g., 131072). Actually, for safety, let me use a chunked approach that works for any N. Algorithm: 1. Each CTA processes one row 2. Chunk the row into BLOCK_N-sized pieces 3. For each chunk, find the top K 4. After all chunks, merge the top-K results But we still need to "store" K results per chunk somewhere. This is the challenge. Alternative: use a running top-K buffer (BLOCK_K = K or padded K). For each chunk: 1. Load chunk 2. Sort chunk 3. Bitonic-merge chunk with running top-K 4. Update running top-K For this to work, BLOCK_K + BLOCK_N should be a power of 2 for efficient sort. If BLOCK_K = K (next power of 2) and BLOCK_N is a power of 2, then BLOCK_K + BLOCK_N may not be a power of 2. E.g., K=64 (power of 2), BLOCK_N=4032 → 4096. But 4032 is not a power of 2. E.g., K=64, BLOCK_N=4096 → 4160. Not power of 2. To make it work: pad both BLOCK_K and BLOCK_N to make sum a power of 2. For example: - BLOCK_K=64, BLOCK_N=4032 → sum=4096, but 4032 not power of 2 - BLOCK_K=128, BLOCK_N=3968 → sum=4096, but 3968 not power of 2 Hmm. Let me think differently. Alternative: sort the chunk and take top K. Then bitonic-merge with running top-K of size K. Since both are sorted desc and have size K, the merge is O(K) work. ```python running_top_v = [-inf, -inf, ...] # size K running_top_i = [-1, -1, ...] # size K for chunk in chunks: # Load chunk v_chunk, i_chunk = load(chunk) # Sort chunk descending v_chunk_sorted, i_chunk_sorted = sort(v_chunk, i_chunk, descending=True) # Take top K of chunk (first K elements of v_chunk_sorted) # Hmm, we need to "extract" first K from BLOCK_N elements # Merge running_top with chunk's top K # Both are sorted desc, size K each # Output: top K of (running_top ∪ chunk_top) = top K of 2K elements # This is a bitonic merge of 2 sorted arrays of size K # Result: 2K elements sorted desc, take first K ... ``` For "extract first K from BLOCK_N sorted": we have the same problem as before. OK let me try yet another approach. Instead of trying to extract K from a sorted BLOCK_N, let me reduce BLOCK_N to K using a "top-K reduction" that doesn't need sort. Approach: "top-K tree reduction" - Compare adjacent pairs, keep the larger - After log2(BLOCK_N/K) levels, we have K elements This is essentially a "selection network" that finds top K in O(N log(N/K)) work. But this is complex to implement in Triton. What if we use a SINGLE Triton sort on a slightly larger buffer? For (1, 131072, 64): we want top 64 of 131072. What if we do this in two stages: - Stage 1: split into 32 chunks of 4096. Each chunk: find top 64 via K iterations of argmax. Total candidates: 32 * 64 = 2048. - Stage 2: find top 64 of 2048. Stage 1: 32 chunks * (64 argmax + 64 mask) of 4096 each = 32 * 64 = 2048 reductions of 4096 elements. Each reduction ~12 levels of tree reduce. Stage 2: similar, 64 reductions of 2048 elements. The argmax can be done with `tl.argmax` which is efficient. Let me code this up. ```python @triton.jit def topk_kernel( x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, N, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base = pid * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') # Load row in chunks # We need to find top K across the entire row # For each chunk, find local top K, then merge # K iterations of argmax on the full row # But we can't store the full row, so we chunk # Strategy: do K iterations, each iteration scans the entire row # But we can't keep the row in registers # Alternative: process all chunks first to find local top K, # then merge local top Ks # Allocate a buffer for K * N_CHUNKS candidates # K = 64, N_CHUNKS = 32, total = 2048 elements # 2048 fits in one block easily ``` Wait, this requires the entire candidate buffer (2048) to be in registers per CTA. That's 2048 floats = 8KB per CTA. Fits. But we need to fill it. For each chunk, we do K iterations of argmax, getting K results, and store them. We can use a (N_CHUNKS, K) buffer. To "store" the K results, we need to write to specific positions. Triton doesn't allow that directly. Hmm. OK, let me use a 2D buffer approach: ```python # Buffer shape (N_CHUNKS, BLOCK_N), 32 * 4096 = 128K floats = 512KB. Doesn't fit. # Or (N_CHUNKS, K), 32 * 64 = 2048 floats = 8KB. Fits. # But we need to write to specific rows dynamically. Hard. ``` Triton has `tl.atomic_add` and `tl.atomic_max` for atomic operations. Actually, the cleanest approach I can think of: for each row, do a single Triton sort of the full row (if BLOCK_N is large enough), then extract top K. The key question: what is the max BLOCK_N that Triton's tl.sort supports? Let me just try it. If it works for 131072, great. If not, we need chunking. Let me write the code and test. ```python """TopK kernel: per-row full sort.""" import torch import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def topk_kernel( x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, N: tl.constexpr, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, NUM_SPLITS: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base = pid * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) i = offs.to(tl.int32) v_sorted, i_sorted = tl.sort(v, i, dim=0, descending=True) # Extract top K via repeated split v_cur = v_sorted i_cur = i_sorted for _ in tl.static_range(NUM_SPLITS): v_first, _ = tl.split(v_cur) i_first, _ = tl.split(i_cur) v_cur = v_first i_cur = i_first k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, v_cur) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, i_cur) def next_pow2(x): p = 1 while p < x: p *= 2 return p def topk(x, k): B, N = x.shape BLOCK_N = next_pow2(N) NUM_SPLITS = int(math.log2(BLOCK_N // k)) if k > 1 else 0 val = torch.empty(B, k, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device) idx = torch.empty(B, k, dtype=torch.int32, device=x.device) grid = (B,) topk_kernel[grid](x, val, idx, N, k, BLOCK_N, NUM_SPLITS) return val, idx ``` Let me also handle k=1 specially: just argmax. And K must be a power of 2 for our split approach. K=1 is OK (NUM_SPLITS = log2(BLOCK_N / 1) = log2(BLOCK_N)). For K=8, 16, 32, 64, all powers of 2, NUM_SPLITS works. Let me write this and test. Chunk the row into pieces of, say, 4096. Process each chunk to find local top-K. Then merge. For "find local top-K" of a chunk: do K iterations of argmax. Store results in shared memory or a 2D tensor. For "merge": do K iterations of argmax on the (N_CHUNKS * K) candidates. Let me code this. ```python @triton.jit def topk_kernel_chunked( x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, N, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, N_CHUNKS: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_CAND: tl.constexpr, # N_CHUNKS * K, padded to power of 2 ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base = pid * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') # Allocate candidate buffer # Shape: (N_CHUNKS * K,) # We need to fill it with local top K of each chunk cand_v = tl.full((BLOCK_CAND,), NEG_INF, tl.float32) cand_i = tl.full((BLOCK_CAND,), -1, tl.int32) # Process each chunk for chunk_idx in range(N_CHUNKS): chunk_start = chunk_idx * BLOCK_N offs = chunk_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) i = offs.to(tl.int32) # Find local top K of this chunk for k in tl.static_range(K): max_idx = tl.argmax(v, axis=0) max_val = tl.max(v) # Store in candidate buffer at position chunk_idx * K + k # We can use atomic operations or a 2D approach # ... ``` The issue: storing at dynamic positions in a 1D buffer. We can use atomic operations on shared memory, but Triton doesn't expose shared memory directly. Alternative: use a 2D buffer (N_CHUNKS, BLOCK_N) where we mark the K selected positions. Then "extract" the K marked positions per row. Or: use tl.where and sum tricks. Let me think. For each chunk, we do K iterations. Each iteration selects a position. We have K positions per chunk. Idea: maintain a "selected" mask per chunk. After K iterations, the K selected positions are marked. We can then "extract" them by using tl.where + sum. But this is complex. Alternative idea: Instead of doing K iterations within a chunk and then merging, do K iterations on the full candidate buffer. The candidate buffer has N_CHUNKS * K entries. We do K iterations of argmax. Total: K * (N_CHUNKS * K) = K² * N_CHUNKS comparisons. For K=64, N_CHUNKS=32: 64² * 32 = 131K comparisons. Not bad. But we need to "fill" the candidate buffer with the local top Ks of each chunk. To do this without dynamic indexing... Hmm. Let me think of another approach. What if for each chunk, we do K iterations of argmax, and immediately merge with the running top K? ```python running_top_v = tl.full((K_PAD,), NEG_INF, tl.float32) # K_PAD = K (power of 2) running_top_i = tl.full((K_PAD,), -1, tl.int32) for chunk_idx in range(N_CHUNKS): # Load chunk v, i = load(chunk) # Find local top K via K iterations local_top_v = tl.full((K_PAD,), NEG_INF, tl.float32) local_top_i = tl.full((K_PAD,), -1, tl.int32) for k in range(K): max_idx = tl.argmax(v, axis=0) max_val = tl.max(v) # ... v = tl.where(... == max_idx, NEG_INF, v) # Merge running_top and local_top # Both sorted desc, size K each # Output: top K of (running_top ∪ local_top) = top K of 2K # ... ``` We still have the issue of "store results in local_top_v[k]". Alternative: don't store local_top_v, just append to running_top via a 2K-element candidate buffer. Actually, here's an idea. The running top-K is maintained as a sorted list of K elements. For each chunk, we have BLOCK_N elements. We want the new running top-K to be the top K of (old running top-K ∪ chunk's BLOCK_N elements). For BLOCK_N + K = power of 2, we can: 1. Hmm, that's a lot. Alternative: don't sort the entire combined. Just do K iterations of argmax on the combined. For each chunk: - Load chunk - For k in K: - max_idx = argmax(chunk) OR argmax(running_top) - max_val = max(chunk) OR max(running_top) - Add to result - Mask out This is K iterations per chunk, each doing 2 reductions (one for chunk, one for running_top, then take overall max). Total: 32 * 64 * 2 = 4096 reductions of 4096 + 64 elements. Hmm, this is awkward. Input: local top-Ks from all chunks for that row. Output: final top-K. For phase 1, we have grid (B, N_CHUNKS). For (1, 131072, 64) with 8 chunks: grid = (1, 8). For phase 2, we have grid (B,). For (1, 131072, 64): grid = (1,). Phase 1: for each (row, chunk), find top K. Can use the chunked approach within a single CTA. Phase 2: for each row, take the (N_CHUNKS * K) candidates, find top K. We can do K iterations of argmax. Let me code this. ```python @triton.jit def topk_phase1_kernel( x_ptr, cand_v_ptr, cand_i_ptr, N, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, N_CHUNKS: tl.constexpr, stride_xb, stride_cb, stride_ci, # strides ): # Each program handles one (row, chunk) pid_b = tl.program_id(0) pid_c = tl.program_id(1) chunk_start = pid_c * BLOCK_N base = pid_b * stride_xb NEG_INF = float('-inf') offs = chunk_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) i = offs.to(tl.int32) # K iterations of argmax k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) # We need to store K results. Use a 2D approach. # ... # For now, let's use tl.sort and extract top K v_sorted, i_sorted = tl.sort(v, i, dim=0, descending=True) # Extract first K via split NUM_SPLITS: tl.constexpr = ... # log2(BLOCK_N // K) v_cur = v_sorted i_cur = i_sorted for _ in tl.static_range(NUM_SPLITS): v_first, _ = tl.split(v_cur) i_first, _ = tl.split(i_cur) v_cur = v_first i_cur = i_first # Write to candidate buffer cand_v_offs = pid_b * stride_cb + pid_c * K + k_offs cand_i_offs = pid_b * stride_ci + pid_c * K + k_offs tl.store(cand_v_ptr + cand_v_offs, v_cur) tl.store(cand_i_ptr + cand_i_offs, i_cur) ``` This works if BLOCK_N is small enough to fit in shared memory for sort. For BLOCK_N=16384: 64KB shared memory needed. Fits in 228KB. For smaller N (8192, 16384, 4096, 12000), we don't need chunking. We can have a single CTA per row. Hmm, this is getting complex. Let me write a simple version and test. ```python """TopK kernel: per-row full sort, single CTA per row.""" import math import torch import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def topk_kernel( x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, N: tl.constexpr, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, NUM_SPLITS: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base = pid * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) i = offs.to(tl.int32) v_sorted, i_sorted = tl.sort(v, i, dim=0, descending=True) v_cur = v_sorted i_cur = i_sorted for _ in tl.static_range(NUM_SPLITS): v_first, _ = tl.split(v_cur) i_first, _ = tl.split(i_cur) v_cur = v_first i_cur = i_first k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, v_cur) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, i_cur) def next_pow2(x): if x <= 1: return 1 return 1 << (x - 1).bit_length() def topk_2d(x, k): B, N = x.shape BLOCK_N = next_pow2(N) K_PAD = next_pow2(k) NUM_SPLITS = int(math.log2(BLOCK_N // K_PAD)) if K_PAD > 1 else int(math.log2(BLOCK_N)) val = torch.empty(B, k, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device) idx = torch.empty(B, k, dtype=torch.int32, device=x.device) grid = (B,) topk_kernel[grid](x, val, idx, N, k, BLOCK_N, NUM_SPLITS, num_warps=8) return val, idx ``` Let me also handle the k=1 case (NUM_SPLITS = log2(BLOCK_N) instead of log2(BLOCK_N // K)). If K=1, K_PAD = 1. Then NUM_SPLITS = log2(BLOCK_N // 1) = log2(BLOCK_N). After NUM_SPLITS splits, we have 1 element (the max). For K=8, K_PAD=8, BLOCK_N=8192: NUM_SPLITS = log2(8192/8) = log2(1024) = 10. After 10 splits, we have 8 elements (the top 8). OK let me also convert the output to int64. Let me write the full solution: ```python """TopK kernel: per-row full bitonic sort.""" import math import torch import torch.nn as nn import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def topk_kernel( x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, N: tl.constexpr, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, NUM_SPLITS: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base = pid * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) i = offs.to(tl.int32) v_sorted, i_sorted = tl.sort(v, i, dim=0, descending=True) v_cur = v_sorted i_cur = i_sorted for _ in tl.static_range(NUM_SPLITS): v_first, _ = tl.split(v_cur) i_first, _ = tl.split(i_cur) v_cur = v_first i_cur = i_first k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, v_cur) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, i_cur) def next_pow2(x): if x <= 1: return 1 return 1 << (x - 1).bit_length() OP_TYPE = "topk" SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["fp32"] HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"] class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, batch: int, n: int, k: int): super().__init__() self.batch, self.n, self.k = batch, n, k self.register_buffer("_dummy", torch.zeros(1)) def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor): B, N = x.shape K = self.k BLOCK_N = next_pow2(N) K_PAD = max(1, next_pow2(K)) NUM_SPLITS = int(math.log2(BLOCK_N // K_PAD)) if BLOCK_N // K_PAD > 1 else 0 val = torch.empty(B, K, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device) idx_i32 = torch.empty(B, K, dtype=torch.int32, device=x.device) # Choose num_warps based on size if BLOCK_N <= 1024: num_warps = 4 elif BLOCK_N <= 8192: num_warps = 8 elif BLOCK_N <= 32768: num_warps = 16 else: num_warps = 32 grid = (B,) topk_kernel[grid](x, val, idx_i32, N, K, BLOCK_N, NUM_SPLITS, num_warps=num_warps) return val, idx_i32.to(torch.int64) batch = 64 n = 8192 k = 8 def get_inputs(): x = torch.randn(batch, n, dtype=torch.float32) return [x] def get_init_inputs(): return [batch, n, k] ``` Let me test this with the small shapes first. The (1, 131072, 64) case will likely fail or be slow, but we can handle it separately. Actually let me check: does Triton support sort on BLOCK_N=131072? Probably not. Let me first test with smaller BLOCK_N. Let me write the code and run it. Actually, let me also check what the max BLOCK_N for tl.sort is. B200 has 228KB shared memory per SM. So this won't work. For BLOCK_N=16384: 64KB. Fits. For BLOCK_N=32768: 128KB. Fits (barely). For (1, 131072, 64), we need a different approach. OK let me code this. ```python """TopK kernel: multi-CTA phase 1 (local top-K) + phase 2 (reduce).""" import math import torch import torch.nn as nn import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def topk_phase1_kernel( x_ptr, cand_v_ptr, cand_i_ptr, N, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, NUM_SPLITS: tl.constexpr, # log2(BLOCK_N // K) ): pid_b = tl.program_id(0) pid_c = tl.program_id(1) chunk_start = pid_c * BLOCK_N base = pid_b * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') offs = chunk_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) i = offs.to(tl.int32) v_sorted, i_sorted = tl.sort(v, i, dim=0, descending=True) v_cur = v_sorted i_cur = i_sorted for _ in tl.static_range(NUM_SPLITS): v_first, _ = tl.split(v_cur) i_first, _ = tl.split(i_cur) v_cur = v_first i_cur = i_first k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) cand_v_offs = (pid_b * tl.num_programs(1) + pid_c) * K + k_offs cand_i_offs = (pid_b * tl.num_programs(1) + pid_c) * K + k_offs tl.store(cand_v_ptr + cand_v_offs, v_cur) tl.store(cand_i_ptr + cand_i_offs, i_cur) @triton.jit def topk_phase2_kernel( cand_v_ptr, cand_i_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, K: tl.constexpr, N_CAND: tl.constexpr, # N_CHUNKS * K, padded to power of 2 NUM_SPLITS: tl.constexpr, # log2(N_CAND // K) ): pid = tl.program_id(0) NEG_INF = float('-inf') cand_offs = tl.arange(0, N_CAND) v = tl.load(cand_v_ptr + pid * N_CAND + cand_offs, other=NEG_INF) i = tl.load(cand_i_ptr + pid * N_CAND + cand_offs, other=-1) v_sorted, i_sorted = tl.sort(v, i, dim=0, descending=True) v_cur = v_sorted i_cur = i_sorted for _ in tl.static_range(NUM_SPLITS): v_first, _ = tl.split(v_cur) i_first, _ = tl.split(i_cur) v_cur = v_first i_cur = i_first k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, v_cur) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, i_cur) def next_pow2(x): if x <= 1: return 1 return 1 << (x - 1).bit_length() def topk_2d(x, k): B, N = x.shape K_PAD = max(1, next_pow2(k)) # Determine BLOCK_N and N_CHUNKS # Goal: BLOCK_N <= some max (e.g., 16384), N_CHUNKS = ceil(N / BLOCK_N) MAX_BLOCK_N = 16384 BLOCK_N = min(MAX_BLOCK_N, next_pow2(N)) N_CHUNKS = (N + BLOCK_N - 1) // BLOCK_N N_CHUNKS_PAD = next_pow2(N_CHUNKS) N_CAND = N_CHUNKS_PAD * K_PAD NUM_SPLITS_1 = int(math.log2(BLOCK_N // K_PAD)) NUM_SPLITS_2 = int(math.log2(N_CAND // K_PAD)) val = torch.empty(B, k, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device) idx_i32 = torch.empty(B, k, dtype=torch.int32, device=x.device) cand_v = torch.empty(B, N_CAND, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device) cand_i = torch.empty(B, N_CAND, dtype=torch.int32, device=x.device) # Phase 1 grid1 = (B, N_CHUNKS) topk_phase1_kernel[grid1]( x, cand_v, cand_i, N, k, BLOCK_N, NUM_SPLITS_1, ) # Phase 2 grid2 = (B,) topk_phase2_kernel[grid2]( cand_v, cand_i, val, idx_i32, k, N_CAND, NUM_SPLITS_2, ) return val, idx_i32.to(torch.int64) ``` Hmm, but for (128, 4096, 1), BLOCK_N=4096, N_CHUNKS=1. OK let me write the code. ```python """TopK kernel: phase 1 (per-chunk sort) + phase 2 (final reduce).""" import math import torch import torch.nn as nn import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def topk_phase1_kernel( x_ptr, cand_v_ptr, cand_i_ptr, N, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, NUM_SPLITS_1: tl.constexpr, ): pid_b = tl.program_id(0) pid_c = tl.program_id(1) chunk_start = pid_c * BLOCK_N base = pid_b * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') offs = chunk_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) i = offs.to(tl.int32) v_sorted, i_sorted = tl.sort(v, i, dim=0, descending=True) v_cur = v_sorted i_cur = i_sorted for _ in tl.static_range(NUM_SPLITS_1): v_first, _ = tl.split(v_cur) i_first, _ = tl.split(i_cur) v_cur = v_first i_cur = i_first k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) n_chunks = tl.num_programs(1) cand_v_offs = (pid_b * n_chunks + pid_c) * K + k_offs cand_i_offs = (pid_b * n_chunks + pid_c) * K + k_offs tl.store(cand_v_ptr + cand_v_offs, v_cur) tl.store(cand_i_ptr + cand_i_offs, i_cur) @triton.jit def topk_phase2_kernel( cand_v_ptr, cand_i_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, K: tl.constexpr, N_CAND: tl.constexpr, NUM_SPLITS_2: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) NEG_INF = float('-inf') cand_offs = tl.arange(0, N_CAND) v = tl.load(cand_v_ptr + pid * N_CAND + cand_offs, other=NEG_INF) i = tl.load(cand_i_ptr + pid * N_CAND + cand_offs, other=-1) v_sorted, i_sorted = tl.sort(v, i, dim=0, descending=True) v_cur = v_sorted i_cur = i_sorted for _ in tl.static_range(NUM_SPLITS_2): v_first, _ = tl.split(v_cur) i_first, _ = tl.split(i_cur) v_cur = v_first i_cur = i_first k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, v_cur) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, i_cur) def next_pow2(x): if x <= 1: return 1 return 1 << (x - 1).bit_length() OP_TYPE = "topk" SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["fp32"] HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"] class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, batch: int, n: int, k: int): super().__init__() self.batch, self.n, self.k = batch, n, k self.register_buffer("_dummy", torch.zeros(1)) def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor): B, N = x.shape K = self.k K_PAD = max(1, next_pow2(K)) MAX_BLOCK_N = 16384 BLOCK_N = min(MAX_BLOCK_N, next_pow2(N)) N_CHUNKS = (N + BLOCK_N - 1) // BLOCK_N N_CHUNKS_PAD = max(1, next_pow2(N_CHUNKS)) N_CAND = N_CHUNKS_PAD * K_PAD NUM_SPLITS_1 = int(math.log2(BLOCK_N // K_PAD)) if BLOCK_N // K_PAD > 1 else 0 NUM_SPLITS_2 = int(math.log2(N_CAND // K_PAD)) if N_CAND // K_PAD > 1 else 0 val = torch.empty(B, K, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device) idx_i32 = torch.empty(B, K, dtype=torch.int32, device=x.device) cand_v = torch.empty(B, N_CAND, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device) cand_i = torch.empty(B, N_CAND, dtype=torch.int32, device=x.device) # Determine num_warps based on BLOCK_N if BLOCK_N <= 2048: num_warps = 4 elif BLOCK_N <= 8192: num_warps = 8 else: num_warps = 16 # Phase 1 grid1 = (B, N_CHUNKS) topk_phase1_kernel[grid1]( x, cand_v, cand_i, N, K_PAD, BLOCK_N, NUM_SPLITS_1, num_warps=num_warps, ) # Phase 2 if N_CHUNKS > 1: num_warps_2 = 4 if N_CAND <= 1024 else 8 grid2 = (B,) topk_phase2_kernel[grid2]( cand_v, cand_i, val, idx_i32, K_PAD, N_CAND, NUM_SPLITS_2, num_warps=num_warps_2, ) else: # Skip phase 2, copy directly k_offs = torch.arange(K, device=x.device) val.copy_(cand_v[:, :K]) idx_i32.copy_(cand_i[:, :K]) return val, idx_i32.to(torch.int64) batch = 64 n = 8192 k = 8 def get_inputs(): x = torch.randn(batch, n, dtype=torch.float32) return [x] def get_init_inputs(): return [batch, n, k] ``` Wait, there's an issue. But we're storing K_PAD in the candidate buffer. The buffer has N_CHUNKS * K_PAD elements. Phase 2 takes top K_PAD. We then output first K of those. For K = K_PAD, this is trivial: store K, take top K, output K. Let me just code for the K = K_PAD case (all our shapes). ```python """TopK kernel: phase 1 (per-chunk sort) + phase 2 (final reduce).""" import math import torch import torch.nn as nn import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def topk_phase1_kernel( x_ptr, cand_v_ptr, cand_i_ptr, N, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, NUM_SPLITS_1: tl.constexpr, ): pid_b = tl.program_id(0) pid_c = tl.program_id(1) chunk_start = pid_c * BLOCK_N base = pid_b * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') offs = chunk_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) i = offs.to(tl.int32) v_sorted, i_sorted = tl.sort(v, i, dim=0, descending=True) v_cur = v_sorted i_cur = i_sorted for _ in tl.static_range(NUM_SPLITS_1): v_first, _ = tl.split(v_cur) i_first, _ = tl.split(i_cur) v_cur = v_first i_cur = i_first k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) n_chunks = tl.num_programs(1) cand_v_offs = (pid_b * n_chunks + pid_c) * K + k_offs cand_i_offs = (pid_b * n_chunks + pid_c) * K + k_offs tl.store(cand_v_ptr + cand_v_offs, v_cur) tl.store(cand_i_ptr + cand_i_offs, i_cur) @triton.jit def topk_phase2_kernel( cand_v_ptr, cand_i_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, K: tl.constexpr, N_CAND: tl.constexpr, NUM_SPLITS_2: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) NEG_INF = float('-inf') cand_offs = tl.arange(0, N_CAND) v = tl.load(cand_v_ptr + pid * N_CAND + cand_offs, other=NEG_INF) i = tl.load(cand_i_ptr + pid * N_CAND + cand_offs, other=-1) v_sorted, i_sorted = tl.sort(v, i, dim=0, descending=True) v_cur = v_sorted i_cur = i_sorted for _ in tl.static_range(NUM_SPLITS_2): v_first, _ = tl.split(v_cur) i_first, _ = tl.split(i_cur) v_cur = v_first i_cur = i_first k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, v_cur) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, i_cur) def next_pow2(x): if x <= 1: return 1 return 1 << (x - 1).bit_length() OP_TYPE = "topk" SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["fp32"] HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"] class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, batch: int, n: int, k: int): super().__init__() self.batch, self.n, self.k = batch, n, k self.register_buffer("_dummy", torch.zeros(1)) def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor): B, N = x.shape K = self.k K_PAD = max(1, next_pow2(K)) MAX_BLOCK_N = 16384 BLOCK_N = min(MAX_BLOCK_N, next_pow2(N)) N_CHUNKS = (N + BLOCK_N - 1) // BLOCK_N N_CHUNKS_PAD = max(1, next_pow2(N_CHUNKS)) N_CAND = N_CHUNKS_PAD * K_PAD NUM_SPLITS_1 = int(math.log2(BLOCK_N // K_PAD)) if BLOCK_N // K_PAD > 1 else 0 NUM_SPLITS_2 = int(math.log2(N_CAND // K_PAD)) if N_CAND // K_PAD > 1 else 0 val = torch.empty(B, K, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device) idx_i32 = torch.empty(B, K, dtype=torch.int32, device=x.device) cand_v = torch.empty(B, N_CAND, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device) cand_i = torch.empty(B, N_CAND, dtype=torch.int32, device=x.device) if BLOCK_N <= 2048: num_warps = 4 elif BLOCK_N <= 8192: num_warps = 8 else: num_warps = 16 # Phase 1 grid1 = (B, N_CHUNKS) topk_phase1_kernel[grid1]( x, cand_v, cand_i, N, K_PAD, BLOCK_N, NUM_SPLITS_1, num_warps=num_warps, ) # Phase 2 if N_CHUNKS > 1: num_warps_2 = 4 if N_CAND <= 1024 else 8 grid2 = (B,) topk_phase2_kernel[grid2]( cand_v, cand_i, val, idx_i32, K_PAD, N_CAND, NUM_SPLITS_2, num_warps=num_warps_2, ) else: # Skip phase 2, copy directly val.copy_(cand_v[:, :K]) idx_i32.copy_(cand_i[:, :K]) return val, idx_i32.to(torch.int64) batch = 64 n = 8192 k = 8 def get_inputs(): x = torch.randn(batch, n, dtype=torch.float32) return [x] def get_init_inputs(): return [batch, n, k] ``` Let me save this and test. First, let me check for any issues. Wait, I see an issue. In the (1, 131072, 64) case: - BLOCK_N = 16384 - N_CHUNKS = 131072 / 16384 = 8 - N_CHUNKS_PAD = 8 - N_CAND = 8 * 64 = 512 - NUM_SPLITS_1 = log2(16384 / 64) = log2(256) = 8 - NUM_SPLITS_2 = log2(512 / 64) = log2(8) = 3 Phase 1: 1*8 = 8 CTAs, each sorts 16384 elements and extracts top 64. Phase 2: 1 CTA, sorts 512 elements and extracts top 64. For the (128, 4096, 1) case: - BLOCK_N = 4096 - N_CHUNKS = 1 - N_CHUNKS_PAD = 1 - N_CAND = 1 * 1 = 1 - NUM_SPLITS_1 = log2(4096 / 1) = 12 - NUM_SPLITS_2 = log2(1 / 1) = 0 Phase 1: 128*1 = 128 CTAs, each sorts 4096 elements and extracts top 1. Phase 2: skipped, just copy. OK this looks right. Let me test. Wait, I notice that for K=1, the tl.sort would have a 1-element output. Is that OK? Let me think. +"""Test tl.topk in Triton 3.6."""
+import triton
+import triton.language as tl
+import torch
+
+
+@triton.jit
+def test_topk(x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, N: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, K: tl.constexpr):
+    offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)
+    mask = offs < N
+    v = tl.load(x_ptr + offs, mask=mask, other=-float('inf'))
+    topk_v, topk_i = tl.topk(v, K)
+    tl.store(val_ptr + tl.arange(0, K), topk_v)
+    tl.store(idx_ptr + tl.arange(0, K), topk_i)
+
+
+def main():
+    x = torch.tensor([3.0, 1.0, 4.0, 1.0, 5.0, 9.0, 2.0, 6.0, 5.0, 3.0, 5.0, 8.0, 9.0, 7.0, 9.0, 3.0], device='cuda')
+    val = torch.empty(4, device='cuda', dtype=torch.float32)
+    idx = torch.empty(4, device='cuda', dtype=torch.int32)
+    test_topk[(1,)](x, val, idx, 16, 16, 4)
+    print('input:', x)
+    print('val:', val)
+    print('idx:', idx)
+
+
+if __name__ == '__main__':
+    main()

reasoning (32749 chars)
Excellent! `tl.topk(x, k, dim=None)` calls `sort_impl(x, k=k, dim=dim, descending=True)`. And it uses the bitonic top-k algorithm from this paper: https://www.doc.ic.ac.uk/~hlgr/pdfs/MassivelyParallelTopK.pdf So `tl.topk` is exactly what we want! It only sorts a single tensor, not a tuple. So we'd need to sort the values and find the indices separately. But the issue is: how do we know which indices correspond to the top values? The current `tl.topk` only returns the top values, not their original indices. One approach: 1. Pack (value, index) into a single int64 via bit manipulation 2. Sort with tl.topk 3. Unpack to get back value and index For float32 with positive values: pack as (value_bits, index) in int64. But float32 can be negative, which complicates bit manipulation. For non-negative float32: bits are in standard IEEE 754 format, and we can use the bits directly as a sort key. For negative values, we need to flip the bits. Standard trick: for IEEE 754 floats, if sign bit is 0 (positive), XOR with 0x80000000 to flip the sign bit. If sign bit is 1 (negative), XOR with 0xFFFFFFFF to invert all bits. Then compare as unsigned ints. ```c uint32_t key = (sign) ? ~bits : bits | 0x80000000; ``` Actually: - For positive floats: bits look like 0XXXXX...X. The sign bit is 0. To make unsigned comparison work, we can just set the sign bit to 0 (XOR with 0x80000000). - Wait, if we set the sign bit to 1 (XOR with 0x80000000), the value is in the upper half of the unsigned range. That works. - For negative floats: bits look like 1XXXXX...X. To make unsigned comparison work, we need to invert all bits. So: ```c uint32_t bits = *(uint32_t*)&f; uint32_t key = (bits & 0x80000000) ? ~bits : (bits | 0x80000000); ``` For descending sort, we want the largest float first. With this encoding, larger keys correspond to larger floats. We sort keys descending, and the top values are the largest floats. Actually for topk descending: we want largest values first. The encoding above makes larger floats have larger unsigned keys. So sort keys descending = sort values descending. OK so the plan: 1. Convert float32 to sortable uint32 (with sign handling) 2. Pack (key, index) into int64: high 32 bits = key, low 32 bits = index 3. Use tl.topk on the int64 packed values 4. Unpack: extract high 32 bits (key) → convert back to float, extract low 32 bits (index) For descending sort, larger keys come first. Wait, there's a subtle issue with negative zero. Let me not worry about that for now. For the int64 packing: high 32 bits = key, low 32 bits = index. We want the key to dominate the comparison. Since int64 comparison is signed and the high 32 bits determine the sign of the int64 (in two's complement), we need to be careful. If key is uint32 and index is uint32, the int64 with key in high bits: for key in [0, 2^31-1], the int64 is positive. For key in [2^31, 2^32-1], the int64 is negative (in two's complement). Hmm, that's a problem. We want key=0 to compare less than key=2^32-1. With the int64 packing, key=2^32-1 would be negative (high bit set), which compares less than 0 in signed. To fix: convert key to int32 (with sign handling) so that larger keys → larger int32. Actually, since we want descending sort, larger values first. If we make larger keys → larger int64 (signed), then sorting descending gives the largest first. For key (uint32, range [0, 2^32-1]), to map to int64 preserving order: - If key < 2^31: int64 = key (positive) - If key >= 2^31: int64 = key - 2^32 (negative) This preserves the ordering. Now sort int64 descending → largest keys first. Wait, this doesn't quite work because int64 with key=2^32-1 is -1, which is the smallest. Sorting descending puts it last, not first. So the packing doesn't work directly. Let me think again. We want the comparison to be based on the key (in unsigned order), and we want the larger key to come first when sorting descending. Option 1: Use a custom comparison function. But Triton's sort doesn't support custom comparators. Option 2: Encode the key such that unsigned comparison = signed comparison. This is only possible if the key range fits in int32 ([-2^31, 2^31-1]) with the largest int32 = largest unsigned. If we map uint32 to int32 with: - uint32 in [0, 2^31-1] → int32 same value (positive) - uint32 in [2^31, 2^32-1] → int32 same value (but stored as int32, this is negative; the bit pattern is the same as uint32 minus 2^32) In int32, [2^31, 2^32-1] becomes [-2^31, -1]. So largest int32 = 2^31 - 1 (corresponds to uint32 = 2^31 - 1, the smallest non-negative value... no wait). Let me think with concrete numbers. uint32 = 0xFFFFFFFF (max), we want this to be the largest when sorting descending. As int32, 0xFFFFFFFF is -1. Sorting descending: 2^31-1 (max int32) > 0 > -1. So -1 is the smallest, not the largest. This approach doesn't work. Option 3: Negate the comparison direction. Pack key such that smaller key (in signed int32) corresponds to larger original value. If original key is uint32 in [0, 2^32-1], we want larger key → smaller int64 (so that sorting descending gives the largest first). For uint32 k: int32 = k - 2^32 (which is always negative). For k=0, int32 = -2^32 which is below int32 range. Doesn't work. OK different approach: use the full 64 bits differently. Option 4: Use the int64 for key comparison only via the high 32 bits, but encode such that the high 32 bits of int64 match the unsigned order. To match unsigned order in int64: we want high 32 bits to be int32 representing the uint32 in a way that larger uint32 → larger int32. This is the same as the original problem. The only way is to map uint32 to int32 in a monotonic way, which only works for the positive range [0, 2^31-1]. So for unsigned uint32 keys, we can't use int64 packing directly. Option 5: Don't use packing. Instead, find the top K values first, then do a second pass to find the indices. Approach: 1. Use tl.topk to find the top K values 2. For each of the K values, do a linear scan (in a separate kernel) to find the matching index This requires 2 kernels, but it's straightforward. Approach 6: Use tl.topk on a tuple of (values, indices) - but we just saw that doesn't work. Let me check tl.topk more carefully. The implementation calls sort_impl which calls _bitonic_merge_hypercube. Maybe it supports tuples. Looking at the code: ```python def sort_impl(x, k, dim, descending): _dim = len(x.shape) - 1 if dim is None else dim ... h = core.reshape(x, [2] * n_dims if n_dims else [1]) for i in core.static_range(1, log_k + 1): h = _bitonic_merge_hypercube(h, i, 2 if i < log_n else descending) for i in core.static_range(log_k + 1, log_n + 1): h = max(h, axis=...) if descending else min(...) h = _bitonic_merge_hypercube(h, log_k, ...) return x ``` This only operates on a single tensor. So tuple support is not there. OK, let me use Approach 5: tl.topk for values, then second pass for indices. Actually, a simpler approach: just call tl.topk on the values, then use the index-finding pass. Or even simpler: use tl.topk on packed int64, where the packing is done with a "monotonic" encoding. Wait, here's an idea: since float32 is only 32 bits and we have 32 bits of int32 for the index, we can pack as int64. The float32 bits, when interpreted as int32 in a specific way, can be sorted correctly. Standard trick: For float32 x, define int32 key as: - If x is positive (sign bit 0): key = bits | 0x80000000 (set the sign bit) - If x is negative (sign bit 1): key = ~bits (invert all bits) This makes the key ordering match the float ordering. For positive floats, larger floats have larger keys. For negative floats, larger floats (less negative) have larger keys. Now we want int64 (key << 32 | index) to sort by key first. The issue: if key is int32 (could be negative), putting it in the high 32 bits of int64 makes the int64 have the same sign as the key. If key is positive, int64 is positive. If key is negative, int64 is negative. For descending sort, we want larger int64 first. Larger positive int64 > smaller positive int64 > negative int64. So all positive keys come before all negative keys. But we want larger float to come first, which corresponds to larger key. If we sort descending, larger positive key comes first (good), then we go down to 0, then to negative keys, then to most-negative key. Wait, descending order means largest first. So: - Largest positive int64: 0x7FFFFFFFFFFFFFFF (key = 0x7FFFFFFF, the largest positive key) - Smallest positive int64: 0x0000000000000000 (key = 0, smallest positive key) - Largest negative int64: 0xFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFF = -1 (key = -1, the largest negative key) - Smallest negative int64: 0x8000000000000000 (key = 0x80000000 = -2^31, smallest key) For descending sort: 0x7FFFFFFFFFFFFFFF > 0x0000000000000000 > -1 > 0x8000000000000000. So the order of keys (descending): 0x7FFFFFFF, 0, -1, 0x80000000. But the original keys in our encoding: largest float → key=0x7FFFFFFF (largest). Then we want this to come first in descending sort. With our packing, it does. Then next largest float → key=0 (smallest positive). Then most negative float (largest negative number) → key=-1. This comes after all positive keys. So the packing works! Let me verify with a small example. Float values: 9.0, -3.0, 5.0 - 9.0: positive, bits = 0x41100000, key = 0xC1100000 (set sign bit). int32 = -1056964608. - -3.0: negative, bits = 0xC0400000, key = ~0xC0400000 = 0x3FBFFFFF. int32 = 0x3FBFFFFF = 1070809087. - 5.0: positive, bits = 0x40A00000, key = 0xC0A00000. int32 = -1063256064. Wait, that's not right. Let me recompute. Float 9.0: - sign = 0 - exp = 130 (biased), actual = 3 - mantissa = 0.125 - bits = 0 10000010 00100000000000000000000 = 0x41100000 - Encoding for sorting: set sign bit = 0xC1100000. As int32 = -1056964608. Float -3.0: - sign = 1 - exp = 128, actual = 1 - mantissa = 0.5 - bits = 1 10000000 10000000000000000000000 = 0xC0400000 - Encoding: invert all bits = 0x3FBFFFFF. As int32 = 1070809087. Float 5.0: - sign = 0 - exp = 129, actual = 2 - mantissa = 0.25 - bits = 0 10000001 01000000000000000000000 = 0x40A00000 - Encoding: set sign bit = 0xC0A00000. As int32 = -1063256064. So keys (as int32): 9.0 → -1056964608, -3.0 → 1070809087, 5.0 → -1063256064. Sorted descending: 1070809087 (-3.0), -1056964608 (9.0), -1063256064 (5.0). But the correct sorted order of values is 9.0, 5.0, -3.0. So the encoding is wrong! Hmm, the issue: my encoding for positive floats doesn't preserve order. Let me re-examine. For positive floats: - bits = 0XXXXX...X (sign = 0, then exp, then mantissa) - Larger positive float → larger bits (since IEEE 754 is monotonic for positives) - We want larger float → larger key (so sort descending gives larger first) - If we set the sign bit, we get 1XXXXX...X. For larger positive float, the bits 0XXXXX...X is larger, so 1XXXXX...X is also larger. So setting the sign bit preserves order for positives. ✓ For negative floats: - bits = 1XXXXX...X (sign = 1, then exp, then mantissa) - For two negative floats, the "more negative" one has smaller magnitude... wait, the bit pattern. Let me think. - In IEEE 754, for negative floats, larger magnitude (more negative) has larger bits (since the sign bit is fixed at 1, and the rest is sorted like a positive float, so larger magnitude → larger exp+mantissa). - So for two negatives, -5.0 has larger bits than -3.0 (because |5| > |3|). - We want -3.0 to come AFTER -5.0 in our sort (since 5.0 > 3.0 > -3.0). Wait, no: 5.0 > 3.0 > -3.0, so 5.0 first, then 3.0, then -3.0. - For -3.0 to be smallest (in our key sense, smallest), we need its key to be smaller than -5.0. - With the encoding ~bits: -3.0 bits = 0xC0400000, ~bits = 0x3FBFFFFF. -5.0 bits = 0xC0A00000, ~bits = 0x3F5FFFFF. So -3.0's key (0x3FBFFFFF) > -5.0's key (0x3F5FFFFF). That means -3.0 is larger than -5.0 in our key, so it would come first. But we want -3.0 to come after -5.0. ✗ The issue: my encoding for negatives is wrong. Let me think again. For negatives: we want -3.0 (larger float) to have LARGER key. So larger float → larger key. For -3.0 and -5.0: -3.0 is the larger float, so its key should be larger. -3.0 bits = 0xC0400000, -5.0 bits = 0xC0A00000. As unsigned, 0xC0A00000 > 0xC0400000. If we invert: ~0xC0400000 = 0x3FBFFFFF, ~0xC0A00000 = 0x3F5FFFFF. As signed int32: 0x3FBFFFFF = 1070809087, 0x3F5FFFFF = 1063256063. 1070809087 > 1063256063. So -3.0's key is larger. ✓ OK my earlier calculation was wrong. Let me redo. Float 9.0: - bits = 0x41100000 (positive) - Set sign bit: 0xC1100000. As int32 = 0xC1100000 = -1056964608. Float -3.0: - bits = 0xC0400000 (negative) - Invert: 0x3FBFFFFF. As int32 = 0x3FBFFFFF = 1070809087. Float 5.0: - bits = 0x40A00000 (positive) - Set sign bit: 0xC0A00000. As int32 = 0xC0A00000 = -1063256064. Sorted descending: 1070809087 (-3.0), -1056964608 (9.0), -1063256064 (5.0). But correct sort is 9.0, 5.0, -3.0. So the order is wrong! Wait, what am I missing. Let me check the ordering of the keys. -3.0's key (1070809087) is positive. 9.0's key (-1056964608) is negative. As int32, positive > negative, so 1070809087 > -1056964608. But the value -3.0 is LESS than 9.0. So our encoding makes -3.0's key LARGER than 9.0's key, which is wrong. The issue: for positives, setting the sign bit makes the key start with bit pattern 1XXX...X. This is in the "negative" range of int32. For negatives, inverting makes the key start with bit pattern 0XXX...X, which is in the "positive" range of int32. So all negative floats have keys in the "positive int32" range, and all positive floats have keys in the "negative int32" range. That's backwards. To fix: swap the encoding. For positives, use the bits directly (positive int32). For negatives, use ~bits (negative int32). Float 9.0: bits = 0x41100000. As int32 = 1094180864. Positive. OK the fundamental issue: any monotonic mapping from float to int32 must be either increasing or decreasing. We can't have a mapping where float ordering matches int32 ordering in a single direction. This is because the floats have a "negative" and "positive" range, and within each range the order is monotonic, but across the ranges (crossing 0), the order reverses. So packing (float, index) into int64 and sorting with signed comparison doesn't work directly. Alternative approaches: 1. Use a 2D sort: sort within "positive" and "negative" groups separately, then merge. 2. Use a 2-kernel approach: first find the K largest values, then find their indices. 3. Approach 2: 2-kernel - Kernel 1: Find the K largest values per row. - Kernel 2: For each (row, k) pair, find the index of the kth largest value. Kernel 1: Just tl.topk on the values. Easy. Kernel 2: For each value in the top K, scan the row and find the matching index. The matching might be ambiguous if there are ties, but for random data ties are rare. Compute: 19M ops, ~75ns. - Kernel 2: 64 CTAs, each scans 131072 elements. 64 * 131072 = 8.4M ops. ~33ns. But launch overhead is ~5μs. Total: dominated by 2 launch overheads = 10μs. Memory: ~100ns. 395	    order_type 0 == ascending
396	    order_type 1 == descending
397	    order_type 2 == alternating
