reasoning (16122 chars)
The Triton bitonic sort uses `x > y` comparisons, which would work for any sortable type (float, int). It doesn't take a tuple directly. So the approach is: 1. Pack (value_bits, index) into int64 in a way that int64 comparison matches float comparison for descending sort. 2. Use tl.topk on the packed int64. But as I analyzed, packing float with index into int64 doesn't preserve float ordering in int64 signed comparison. Alternative: Do TWO sorts. One for positive values, one for negative values. Or: just sort the values and find indices separately. Actually, the simplest approach is: 1. Use tl.topk on the values (just the float values, no indices). 2. For each top-K value, do a separate search to find the index. This requires 2 kernel launches. The first one is fast (just sort). The second is also fast (parallel scan). Let me also consider: for each row, we have a (B, N) tensor. We can use tl.topk to get the top K values per row. The output shape is (B, K). For finding indices, we can use a separate kernel. For each (row, k), we have a target value v. We scan the row to find an index where x[batch, index] == v. For random data, this is unique. For ties, any matching index is OK (per problem statement). Alternatively, we can use a "fuzzy match" with a tolerance. OK let me code this up. Actually wait, there's another approach: use a 2D sort with the value and index as separate "columns". But Triton's sort is 1D (along the last axis). We could reshape (B, N) to (B*N) and sort, but then we lose row boundaries. Hmm. Let me think. OK let me just try the 2-kernel approach: 1. Kernel 1: tl.topk to find top K values per row. 2. Kernel 2: For each (row, k), find the index of that value. For kernel 2, we can do parallel scan: for each (row, k), multiple threads search the row in parallel, then reduce to find the matching index. Or simpler: a single thread does the search, but that's slow for large N. Actually, for kernel 2, we can just do another tl.topk: pack (value, index) into int64 where the comparison is by value first, then by index. This way, ties are broken by index. For the packing: encode float as uint32 (using the standard monotonic encoding), then pack (value_key, index) such that the comparison is by value_key first. For uint32 keys in [0, 2^32-1], we want to compare by them first. We can shift the value_key to the high 32 bits and put the index in the low 32 bits. But as I analyzed, int64 signed comparison doesn't directly correspond to uint32 unsigned comparison for the high bits. Solution: use a mapping that converts uint32 keys to int32 keys monotonically. For uint32 in [0, 2^32-1], we want a mapping m: [0, 2^32-1] → [-2^31, 2^31-1] that preserves order. We can use: m(k) = k - 2^31 (signed shift). For k in [0, 2^31-1], m(k) is in [-2^31, -1]. For k in [2^31, 2^32-1], m(k) is in [0, 2^31-1]. So for k=0 (smallest uint32), m(k) = -2^31 (smallest int32). For k=2^32-1 (largest uint32), m(k) = 2^31-1 (largest int32). Order preserved. ✓ Now for our float-to-uint32 encoding: - Positive: key = bits | 0x80000000 (in [0x80000000, 0xFFFFFFFF] for finite positives) - Negative: key = ~bits (in [0, 0x7FFFFFFF] for finite negatives) For uint32 keys in [0, 0xFFFFFFFF], m(k) = k - 2^31 (signed) is in [-2^31, 2^31-1]. As int32: - k=0 → m=-2^31 (smallest int32) - k=0x80000000 → m=0 - k=0xFFFFFFFF → m=2^31-1 (largest int32) In int64 packed as (m(k) << 32) | index, the high 32 bits are the mapped key. Signed int64 comparison by high bits gives the order of m(k), which gives the order of k, which gives the order of the float (descending for k larger first). For tl.topk descending: we get the largest k first, which is the largest float first. ✓ Wait, let me double check. For tl.topk(x, k, descending=True), it sorts x descending. The largest values come first. For int64 packed as (m(k) << 32) | index, the largest int64 (in signed comparison) corresponds to the largest m(k), which is the largest k, which is the largest float (after encoding). For descending sort, largest first. So the first element of the top K is the largest float. ✓ So the packing is: 1. Encode float → uint32 key (using monotonic encoding) 2. Map uint32 → int32: m(k) = k - 2^31 (signed shift) 3. Pack into int64: packed = (m(k) << 32) | index (as int64) 4. tl.topk on int64 descending → top K floats 5. Unpack: float = unmap_key(packed >> 32), index = packed & 0xFFFFFFFF But wait, when we shift m(k) << 32, we're doing arithmetic shift, which preserves the sign. So for negative m(k), the high bits are all 1s, and the int64 is negative. For descending sort, larger int64 first. Largest int64 = 0x7FFFFFFFFFFFFFFF (m(k) = 0x7FFFFFFF, k = 0xFFFFFFFF, the largest float). Smallest int64 = 0x8000000000000000 (m(k) = 0x80000000 = -2^31, k = 0, smallest float). Wait, m(k) is int32. When we do m(k) << 32 in int64, the result is sign-extended. So m(k) = 0x7FFFFFFF gives int64 = 0x7FFFFFFF00000000. m(k) = 0x80000000 (=-2^31) gives int64 = 0x8000000000000000. m(k) = -1 gives int64 = 0xFFFFFFFF00000000. m(k) = -2^31 gives int64 = 0x8000000000000000. So: - m(k) = 2^31 - 1 (k = 2^32-1, max uint32, max float) → int64 = 0x7FFFFFFF00000000 - m(k) = 0 (k = 2^31, smallest positive finite float max) → int64 = 0x0000000000000000 - m(k) = -1 (k = 2^31 - 1, just below 2^31, largest negative float) → int64 = 0xFFFFFFFF00000000 - m(k) = -2^31 (k = 0, smallest uint32) → int64 = 0x8000000000000000 For descending sort: 0x7FFFFFFF00000000 > 0x0000000000000000 > 0xFFFFFFFF00000000 > 0x8000000000000000. So the order of m(k) (descending): 2^31-1, 0, -1, -2^31. Order of k (descending): 2^32-1, 2^31, 2^31-1, 0. For our float encoding: - k = 0xFFFFFFFF: doesn't correspond to a normal float (this is NaN territory) - k = 0xFF7FFFFF: max_finite (largest positive finite float) - k = 0x80800000: smallest positive subnormal - k = 0x80000000: 0.0 or 0x80000001 = smallest positive subnormal (subnormals have exp=0, mantissa != 0) Hmm wait, let me reconsider. The float-to-uint32 encoding for positive floats is "set sign bit" (bits | 0x80000000). For 0.0 (bits = 0x00000000), the encoded key is 0x80000000. For smallest positive subnormal (bits = 0x00000001), encoded key is 0x80000001. For max_finite (bits = 0x7F7FFFFF), encoded key is 0xFF7FFFFF. For negative floats, encoding is ~bits. For -0.0 (bits = 0x80000000), encoded key is 0x7FFFFFFF. For largest negative finite (bits = 0x80000001, which is -denormal), encoded key is 0x7FFFFFFE. For -max_finite (bits = 0xFF7FFFFF), encoded key is 0x00800000. So for descending order of floats (max → min): max_finite (0xFF7FFFFF), ..., smallest positive (0x80000001), 0.0 (0x80000000), -0.0 (0x7FFFFFFF), ..., -max_finite (0x00800000). In m(k) terms: - 0xFF7FFFFF → m = 0x7F7FFFFF = 2139095039 - 0x80000001 → m = 0x00000001 = 1 - 0x80000000 → m = 0x00000000 = 0 - 0x7FFFFFFF → m = 0xFFFFFFFF (or -1 as signed) - 0x00800000 → m = 0x80800000 (or -2139095040 as signed) Sort m(k) descending: 2139095039 > 1 > 0 > -1 > -2139095040. In int64 signed: 0x7F7FFFFF00000000 > 0x0000000100000000 > 0 > 0xFFFFFFFF00000000 > 0x8080000000000000. Order: max_finite, ..., smallest positive, 0.0, -0.0, ..., -max_finite. ✓ This matches the correct float descending order! So the encoding works. Let me code it up. Implementation in Triton: ```python @triton.jit def encode_float_to_uint32(f): bits = f.to(tl.uint32, bitcast=True) sign = bits & 0x80000000 # If sign is set (negative): invert all bits # If sign is not set (positive): set the sign bit key = tl.where(sign != 0, ~bits, bits | 0x80000000) return key @triton.jit def pack_to_int64(v, i): key = encode_float_to_uint32(v) # Map uint32 to int32: m(k) = k - 2^31 (signed shift) # In int32: m = key - 0x80000000 (but need to handle as signed) # Or: m = key ^ 0x80000000 (XOR with sign bit) - this maps uint32 to int32 # Let's check: k=0 → m=0x80000000=-2^31 ✓ # k=0xFFFFFFFF → m=0x7FFFFFFF=2^31-1 ✓ # k=0x80000000 → m=0 ✓ # Order preserved: if k1 < k2, then k1^0x80000000 < k2^0x80000000 in signed comparison m = key ^ 0x80000000 # As int32 (sign bit flips) # Pack into int64: (m << 32) | index # But m is int32, need to extend to int64 m_ext = m.to(tl.int64) packed = (m_ext << 32) | i.to(tl.int64) return packed ``` Wait, in Triton, can we do these bit operations on float32 directly? `f.to(tl.uint32, bitcast=True)` - reinterpret float32 bits as uint32. This is supported in Triton I believe. Let me check. Actually, let me look at the Triton source to see if there's a bitcast or reinterpret op. Triton has `cast(x, dtype, fp_downcast_rounding=None, bitcast=False)`. So we can do `tl.cast(f, tl.uint32, bitcast=True)`. But the simpler way: just use Python-level operations? No, we need it in the kernel. Let me just try. Actually I realize there might be issues with negative numbers in tl.uint32 etc. Let me just code it up and test. Actually, here's a much simpler approach: don't pack into int64. Use TWO separate operations: 1. Use tl.topk on the original values (just floats) to find the top K values. 2. Use a separate kernel (or the same kernel) to find the indices of those values. The advantage: tl.topk on floats works directly, no encoding needed. The disadvantage: 2 kernel launches. For our use case (top K with K up to 64), the launch overhead is the main cost. 2 launches * 5μs = 10μs. Plus the actual work which is small. Hmm, 10μs is quite a lot. For the (1, 131072, 64) case, the theoretical minimum is ~100ns. 10μs is 100x off. Let me think if there's a way to avoid the 2nd launch. Alternative: Use a 2-step process within a single kernel: - Step 1: find top K values via tl.topk - Step 2: for each (row, k), find the index But step 2 is data-dependent on step 1, so it's hard to do in a single kernel without intermediate global memory. Unless we use cooperative groups or grid-wide synchronization, which Triton doesn't support directly. OK, let me try the packing approach with int64. Let me code it up. Actually, I just realized: for the problem shapes, we have N up to 131072. The values are N=131072 floats. The top K=64 values are the largest. To find their indices, we need to know which positions they came from. If we use the int64 packing with the encoding I described, we can do tl.topk on int64 and get both values and indices in one go. Let me code this. ```python @triton.jit def topk_packed_kernel( x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, N: tl.constexpr, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, NUM_SPLITS: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base = pid * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') # Load row offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) # Pack (v, index) into int64 # Encode float to uint32, then to int32 (XOR with 0x80000000), then to int64 (high 32 bits), OR with index (low 32 bits) bits = v.to(tl.uint32, bitcast=True) sign = bits & 0x80000000 # key: for negative, invert; for positive, set sign bit key = tl.where(sign != 0, ~bits, bits | 0x80000000) # Convert key to int32 by XOR with 0x80000000 (flip sign bit interpretation) # This maps uint32 [0, 2^32-1] to int32 [-2^31, 2^31-1] preserving order m = key ^ 0x80000000 # Now in int32 # Pack into int64: high 32 bits = m, low 32 bits = index m_ext = m.to(tl.int64) packed = (m_ext << 32) | offs.to(tl.int64) # Top K topk_packed = tl.topk(packed, K) # Unpack # Extract low 32 bits (index) and high 32 bits (key) # topk_packed is int64 # index = topk_packed & 0xFFFFFFFF # m = (topk_packed >> 32) as int32 # key = m ^ 0x80000000 (back to uint32) # float = unmap_key_to_float(key) idx = topk_packed & 0xFFFFFFFF m_back = (topk_packed >> 32).to(tl.int32) key_back = m_back ^ tl.constexpr(0x80000000) # int32 XOR # Reconstruct float from key # For key >= 0x80000000: original was positive, key = bits | 0x80000000 → bits = key # For key < 0x80000000: original was negative, key = ~bits → bits = ~key # But we're working in int32. Let's convert to uint32. key_u = key_back.to(tl.uint32) sign_back = key_u & 0x80000000 bits_back = tl.where(sign_back != 0, key_u, ~key_u) val = bits_back.to(tl.float32, bitcast=True) k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, val) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, idx.to(tl.int64)) ``` Hmm, this is getting complex. And the bit manipulation in Triton might not work directly. Let me think of an alternative: maybe use a 2D structure where the value and index are stored separately, and we do the sort using a custom comparison. But Triton's sort doesn't support custom comparisons. OK let me just try the int64 packing approach. If the bit operations don't work, I'll fall back to the 2-kernel approach. Actually, you know what, let me try a completely different approach: use a hand-written CUDA C++ kernel via load_inline. We can implement a custom bitonic top-K that tracks (value, index) pairs. In CUDA C++: 1. Each block processes one row 2. Use shared memory to store the row 3. Do a bitonic top-K (partial bitonic sort) to find the top K 4. Output values and indices The advantage: full control over the bit operations and comparisons. Let me think about this. For (1, 131072, 64), we can't fit 131072 floats in shared memory. We need chunking. Multi-block approach for large N: - Phase 1: each block handles a chunk, finds local top K - Phase 2: reduce across blocks For the per-block top K, we can use a "bitonic top-K" that only sorts the top K. This is more efficient than full sort. The bitonic top-K algorithm (from the paper cited in Triton): 1. Run a bitonic sort up to the K-th level 2. Then for each subsequent level, take the max of the "right" half, merge with the "left" half The result: the first K elements are sorted descending, and the rest are smaller than the K-th element. This is O(N log²K) work, which is much less than O(N log²N) for full sort. For K=64, log²K = 36. For N=131072, log²N = 289. So bitonic top-K is 8x less work than full sort. But implementing this in CUDA is complex. Let me think if there's a simpler approach. Alternative: Use Triton with the 2-kernel approach. For the (1, 131072, 64) case, 2 kernels * 5μs launch overhead = 10μs. That's actually not bad for the small case. But for the (64, 8192, 8) case, 2 kernels * 5μs = 10μs, and the actual work is much smaller. So the launch overhead dominates. Hmm, let me think about how to avoid the 2nd kernel. Idea: Pack value and index into a single int64 using a different encoding, then sort. Let me try a cleaner encoding. Encoding for float v with index i (where index is uint32): - Compute v_bits = v as uint32 - Compute sort_key from v_bits using the monotonic encoding - pack = (sort_key << 32) | i If sort_key is int32 (after the XOR with 0x80000000 to make it signed), then pack as int64: - pack = (int64(sort_key) << 32) | int64(i) For descending sort by int64: largest int64 first. The high 32 bits of pack are sort_key (sign-extended to int64). Larger sort_key → larger int64 (signed). And larger sort_key corresponds to larger float. ✓ Let me write this in Triton. Actually, you know, let me just try the simplest thing: use tl.topk on float values, then find indices in a second kernel. This should work and is simple. But there's another issue: for the (1, 131072, 64) case, tl.topk on 131072 floats might not work due to memory constraints. Let me check: tl.topk uses bitonic sort. For BLOCK_N=131072, the shared memory needed is 131072 * 4 = 512KB. B200 has 228KB. So it won't fit. So we need chunking for (1, 131072, 64). OK plan: 1. +"""Test what BLOCK_N tl.topk supports."""
+import triton
+import triton.language as tl
+import torch
+
+
+@triton.jit
+def topk_test(x_ptr, val_ptr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, K: tl.constexpr):
+    offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)
+    v = tl.load(x_ptr + offs)
+    topk_v = tl.topk(v, K)
+    tl.store(val_ptr + tl.arange(0, K), topk_v)
+
+
+def test_block_n(block_n, k=8, num_warps=8):
+    x = torch.randn(block_n, device='cuda', dtype=torch.float32)
+    val = torch.empty(k, device='cuda', dtype=torch.float32)
+    try:
+        topk_test[(1,)](x, val, BLOCK_N=block_n, K=k, num_warps=num_warps)
+        torch.cuda.synchronize()
+        return True
+    except Exception as e:
+        return f"FAIL: {type(e).__name__}: {str(e)[:200]}"
+
+
+if __name__ == '__main__':
+    for n in [128, 512, 2048, 4096, 8192, 16384, 32768, 65536]:
+        for nw in [4, 8, 16, 32]:
+            r = test_block_n(n, num_warps=nw)
+            print(f"BLOCK_N={n:>6} num_warps={nw:>3} -> {r}")

reasoning (1546 chars)
OK, tl.gather exists. So we can: 1. Use tl.topk on values to get the top K sorted values 2. Use tl.gather to find the indices of those values in the original array But tl.gather finds the values at the given indices, not the other way around. We want: given a target value, find the index. Hmm, we need "inverse gather" or "search". Actually, here's a clean approach: 1. Pack (value_bits, index) into int64 using a clever encoding 2. Use tl.topk to find top K (in terms of value first, then index for ties) 3. Unpack to get back values and indices For the encoding, we need: - Larger float → larger int64 - For ties, smaller index → larger int64 (to get deterministic ordering) Encoding: 1. Float to uint32: standard monotonic encoding (positive: bits | 0x80000000, negative: ~bits) 2. uint32 to int32: XOR with 0x80000000 (to make signed) 3. Pack: int64 = (int32_to_int64(key) << 32) | (index & 0xFFFFFFFF) For descending sort by int64 signed: - Larger int64 first - High 32 bits determine order first - For ties in high 32 bits, low 32 bits determine (larger low bits = larger int64, but we want smaller index first for ties... hmm) Actually, for ties in value, we don't care about the index (per the problem). So we can ignore tie-breaking. For ties in value: the int64 will differ in the low 32 bits (index). The sort is stable, so within tied values, smaller index comes first. Or maybe not stable. Let me not worry about it. OK let me just try the packing approach. In Triton, can we do bitwise operations on float32? Let me test.

+"""Test bit manipulation in Triton for int64 packing."""
+import triton
+import triton.language as tl
+import torch
+
+
+@triton.jit
+def pack_test(x_ptr, out_ptr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr):
+    offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)
+    v = tl.load(x_ptr + offs)
+    # Bitcast float32 to uint32
+    bits = v.to(tl.uint32, bitcast=True)
+    # Compute key: if sign bit set, invert; else set sign bit
+    sign = bits & 0x80000000
+    key = tl.where(sign != 0, ~bits, bits | 0x80000000)
+    # Map uint32 to int32 by XOR with 0x80000000
+    m = (key ^ 0x80000000).to(tl.int32)
+    # Pack into int64
+    m_ext = m.to(tl.int64)
+    packed = (m_ext << 32) | offs.to(tl.int64)
+    tl.store(out_ptr + offs, packed)
+
+
+def main():
+    x = torch.tensor([9.0, -3.0, 5.0, 1.0, -1.0, 0.0, -9.0, 3.0], device='cuda', dtype=torch.float32)
+    out = torch.empty(8, device='cuda', dtype=torch.int64)
+    pack_test[(1,)](x, out, BLOCK_N=8, num_warps=1)
+    print('input:', x)
+    print('packed (int64):', out)
+    # Expected: 9.0 should be largest, then 5.0, 3.0, 1.0, 0.0, -1.0, -3.0, -9.0
+    # In int64 sort descending: 9.0 first, -9.0 last
+    for i in range(8):
+        print(f'  [{i}] v={x[i].item():>5.1f} packed=0x{out[i].item():016X}')
+
+
+if __name__ == '__main__':
+    main()

Let me code it. ```python """TopK kernel: pack (value, index) into int64 for topk.""" import math import torch import torch.nn as nn import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def _pack_val_idx(v, i): """Pack float value and index into int64 such that int64 descending sort corresponds to float descending sort.""" # Bitcast float to uint32 bits = v.to(tl.uint32, bitcast=True) # Compute monotonic key sign = bits & 0x80000000 # If sign is set (negative): invert all bits # If sign is not set (positive): set the sign bit key = tl.where(sign != 0, ~bits, bits | 0x80000000) # Map uint32 [0, 2^32-1] to int32 [-2^31, 2^31-1] preserving order m = (key ^ 0x80000000).to(tl.int32) # Pack: high 32 bits = m, low 32 bits = index m_ext = m.to(tl.int64) return (m_ext << 32) | i.to(tl.int64) @triton.jit def _unpack_val_idx(packed): """Unpack int64 into float value and int index.""" # Extract low 32 bits (index) idx = (packed & 0xFFFFFFFF).to(tl.int64) # Extract high 32 bits (m as int32) m_back = (packed >> 32).to(tl.int32) # Convert back to uint32 key key = (m_back ^ 0x80000000).to(tl.uint32) # Reconstruct float from key sign_back = key & 0x80000000 # If sign_back set: original was positive, bits = key # If sign_back not set: original was negative, bits = ~key bits = tl.where(sign_back != 0, key, ~key) val = bits.to(tl.float32, bitcast=True) return val, idx @triton.jit def topk_kernel( x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, N, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, NUM_SPLITS: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base = pid * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) i = offs.to(tl.int64) # Pack packed = _pack_val_idx(v, i) # Top K topk_packed = tl.topk(packed, K) # Unpack val, idx = _unpack_val_idx(topk_packed) k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, val) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, idx) # ... rest of the code ``` Wait, there's a problem. tl.topk returns a tensor of size K. The packed values are int64. After topk, we have K int64 values. Unpacking gives K floats and K int64s. For the store: val is float32, idx is int64. We can store them directly. But wait, the indices output should be int64 (matching torch.topk). Let me make sure the store uses int64. Actually, for the (1, 131072, 64) case, BLOCK_N=131072 doesn't work. We need chunking. Let me write the chunked version. ```python @triton.jit def topk_phase1_kernel( x_ptr, cand_ptr, # cand: (B, N_CHUNKS * K) int64 packed N, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, N_CHUNKS: tl.constexpr, NUM_SPLITS: tl.constexpr, # log2(BLOCK_N // K) for in-block topk ): pid_b = tl.program_id(0) pid_c = tl.program_id(1) chunk_start = pid_c * BLOCK_N base = pid_b * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') offs = chunk_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) i = offs.to(tl.int64) packed = _pack_val_idx(v, i) # Top K of this chunk topk_packed = tl.topk(packed, K) # Store in candidate buffer # Layout: (B, N_CHUNKS, K) flattened as (B, N_CHUNKS * K) cand_offs = (pid_b * N_CHUNKS + pid_c) * K + tl.arange(0, K) tl.store(cand_ptr + cand_offs, topk_packed) @triton.jit def topk_phase2_kernel( cand_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, K: tl.constexpr, N_CAND: tl.constexpr, # N_CHUNKS * K, padded to power of 2 NUM_SPLITS: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) cand_offs = pid * N_CAND + tl.arange(0, N_CAND) # Load with -inf mask for unused cand = tl.load(cand_ptr + cand_offs) # The unused slots are uninitialized, but should be -inf equivalent # Actually, the encoding for -inf is ... let me think # -inf as float: bits = 0xFF800000 # sign = 0x80000000, key = ~bits = 0x007FFFFF, m = 0x807FFFFF # packed = 0x807FFFFF00000000 # So unused slots should have 0x807FFFFF00000000 (or anything smaller than real values) # Top K topk_packed = tl.topk(cand, K) val, idx = _unpack_val_idx(topk_packed) k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, val) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, idx) ``` Hmm, the issue with phase 2 is that the unused slots in the candidate buffer need to be "small" (i.e., not in the top K). The simplest way: fill the buffer with the "smallest possible" int64 value (i.e., 0x8000000000000000 = -2^63). In the pack encoding, what's the smallest int64? It would correspond to the most negative float. For -inf, the int64 is 0x807FFFFF00000000 (let me recompute). -inf: bits = 0xFF800000. sign = 0x80000000. sign != 0, so key = ~bits = 0x007FFFFF. m = key ^ 0x80000000 = 0x807FFFFF. As int32, 0x807FFFFF = -2139095041. m.to(int64) = 0xFFFFFFFF807FFFFF. << 32 = 0x807FFFFF00000000. | index = 0x807FFFFF00000000 | index. So -inf's packed value is 0x807FFFFF00000000 | index. As int64, this is negative. For "smaller than -inf": we want packed < 0x807FFFFF00000000 | index. In signed int64, this means more negative. For the unused slots, we should fill with a value smaller than any real packed value. The smallest real packed value (most negative int64) corresponds to the most negative float, which is -inf. So we can fill with -inf (0x807FFFFF00000000 | anything). Or we can fill with the int64 minimum: 0x8000000000000000. Either works. Let me use 0x8000000000000000 (the int64 min) for unused slots. N_CAND = 8 * 64 = 512. N_CHUNKS = 8. So N_CHUNKS * K = 512 = N_CAND. No padding needed. For (1, 131072, 64) with N_CHUNKS=8: N_CAND = 512, no padding. Actually, to be efficient, let me skip phase 2 when N_CHUNKS=1 (use phase 1 to write directly to output). ```python class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, batch, n, k): super().__init__() self.batch, self.n, self.k = batch, n, k self.register_buffer("_dummy", torch.zeros(1)) def forward(self, x): B, N = x.shape K = self.k MAX_BLOCK_N = 16384 # or whatever BLOCK_N = min(MAX_BLOCK_N, next_pow2(N)) N_CHUNKS = (N + BLOCK_N - 1) // BLOCK_N N_CHUNKS_PAD = next_pow2(N_CHUNKS) K_PAD = max(1, next_pow2(K)) N_CAND = N_CHUNKS_PAD * K_PAD val = torch.empty(B, K, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device) idx = torch.empty(B, K, dtype=torch.int64, device=x.device) if N_CHUNKS == 1: # Single CTA per row, direct output cand_int64 = torch.empty(B, K_PAD, dtype=torch.int64, device=x.device) grid = (B,) topk_kernel[grid](x, cand_int64, N, K, BLOCK_N, K_PAD, num_warps=...) # cand_int64 is packed, need to unpack # Or write a kernel that outputs val and idx directly ... else: # Multi-CTA cand_int64 = torch.empty(B, N_CAND, dtype=torch.int64, device=x.device) # Phase 1 grid1 = (B, N_CHUNKS) topk_phase1_kernel[grid1](x, cand_int64, N, K, BLOCK_N, N_CHUNKS, K_PAD, num_warps=...) # Phase 2 grid2 = (B,) topk_phase2_kernel[grid2](cand_int64, val, idx, K, N_CAND, K_PAD, num_warps=...) return val, idx ``` Hmm, the unpack can be done in a separate kernel. Let me think. For simplicity, let me always do phase 1 + phase 2, and have phase 2 also do the unpack. Actually, let me write a single kernel that does both: load chunk, find top K, merge with running top K, output. But the "merge with running top K" is the tricky part because we need to track indices. OK let me just do the 2-phase approach. For phase 1, the output is packed (int64). For phase 2, the input is packed, the output is unpacked (val, idx). Let me code it. ```python import math import torch import torch.nn as nn import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def _pack(v, i): """Pack float value and index into int64.""" bits = v.to(tl.uint32, bitcast=True) sign = bits & 0x80000000 key = tl.where(sign != 0, ~bits, bits | 0x80000000) m = (key ^ 0x80000000).to(tl.int32) m_ext = m.to(tl.int64) return (m_ext << 32) | i.to(tl.int64) @triton.jit def _unpack(packed): """Unpack int64 into float value and int index.""" idx = (packed & 0xFFFFFFFF).to(tl.int64) m_back = (packed >> 32).to(tl.int32) key = (m_back ^ 0x80000000).to(tl.uint32) sign_back = key & 0x80000000 bits = tl.where(sign_back != 0, key, ~key) val = bits.to(tl.float32, bitcast=True) return val, idx @triton.jit def topk_phase1_kernel( x_ptr, cand_ptr, N, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, N_CHUNKS: tl.constexpr, ): pid_b = tl.program_id(0) pid_c = tl.program_id(1) chunk_start = pid_c * BLOCK_N base = pid_b * N NEG_INF = float('-inf') offs = chunk_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF) i = offs.to(tl.int64) packed = _pack(v, i) # Top K of this chunk topk_packed = tl.topk(packed, K) # Store in candidate buffer cand_offs = (pid_b * N_CHUNKS + pid_c) * K + tl.arange(0, K) tl.store(cand_ptr + cand_offs, topk_packed) @triton.jit def topk_phase2_kernel( cand_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, K: tl.constexpr, N_CAND: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) cand_offs = pid * N_CAND + tl.arange(0, N_CAND) cand = tl.load(cand_ptr + cand_offs) # Top K topk_packed = tl.topk(cand, K) val, idx = _unpack(topk_packed) k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, val) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, idx) def next_pow2(x): if x <= 1: return 1 return 1 << (x - Let me try this. ```python @triton.jit def topk_single_kernel( x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, N: tl.constexpr, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, N_CHUNKS: tl.constexpr, NEG_INF: tl.constexpr, # = -inf as int64 packed ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base = pid * N NEG_INF_F = float('-inf') # Initialize running top K # Use -inf as initial value NEG_INF_PACKED: tl.constexpr = ... # -inf packed running_packed = tl.full((K,), NEG_INF_PACKED, tl.int64) for chunk_idx in range(N_CHUNKS): chunk_start = chunk_idx * BLOCK_N offs = chunk_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF_F) i = offs.to(tl.int64) packed = _pack(v, i) chunk_top = tl.topk(packed, K) # Merge running_packed and chunk_top (both sorted desc, size K each) # Concatenate and topk merged = tl.cat(running_packed, chunk_top) # size 2K running_packed = tl.topk(merged, K) # Unpack and store val, idx = _unpack(running_packed) k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, val) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, idx) ``` This is a single kernel with no global memory between chunks. Let me code it up. ```python @triton.jit def topk_single_kernel( x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr, N, K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, N_CHUNKS: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base = pid * N NEG_INF_F = float('-inf') NEG_INF_I64 = -9223372036854775808 # int64 min # Initialize running top K with -inf running_packed = tl.full((K,), NEG_INF_I64, tl.int64) for chunk_idx in range(N_CHUNKS): chunk_start = chunk_idx * BLOCK_N offs = chunk_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) mask = offs < N v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF_F) i = offs.to(tl.int64) packed = _pack(v, i) chunk_top = tl.topk(packed, K) # Merge: cat [running, chunk_top], top K merged = tl.cat(running_packed, chunk_top) running_packed = tl.topk(merged, K) # Unpack and store val, idx = _unpack(running_packed) k_offs = tl.arange(0, K) tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, val) tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, idx) ``` This is a single kernel that handles all cases. The loop runs N_CHUNKS times. For N_CHUNKS=1, the loop runs once, and the merge is trivial (NEG_INF + chunk_top → top K = chunk_top). For the (1, 131072, 64) case, N_CHUNKS=8, the loop runs 8 times. Each iteration does: - Load 16384 elements - Pack - Topk K=64 - Merge with running (cat 128 elements, topk 64) - Update running The merge (cat + topk) is O(128 * log²(128)) ≈ 128 * 49 = 6K ops. +"""Top-K kernel via bitonic top-k with in-register merge.
+
+Approach:
+  1. Pack (value, index) into int64 with a monotonic float encoding so that
+     int64 descending order == float descending order.
+  2. Per row: load BLOCK_N elements, find top-K, then iteratively merge with
+     a running top-K buffer (all in registers via tl.cat + tl.topk). After
+     all chunks, unpack and write the result.
+
+Shapes:
+  - (1, 131072, 64): single row, 8 chunks of 16384, in-register merge.
+  - (64, 8192, 8): one CTA per row, one chunk of 8192.
+  - (32, 16384, 32): one CTA per row, one chunk of 16384.
+  - (16, 12000, 16): one CTA per row, one chunk of 16384 (with mask).
+  - (128, 4096, 1): one CTA per row, one chunk of 4096.
+"""
+import math
+
+import torch
+import torch.nn as nn
+import triton
+import triton.language as tl
+
+
+@triton.jit
+def _pack(v, i):
+    """Pack (float value, int index) into int64.
+
+    Float-to-uint32 monotonic encoding:
+      - For positive floats, set the sign bit (bits | 0x80000000).
+      - For negative floats, invert all bits (~bits).
+    This makes larger floats have larger unsigned keys.
+
+    Then uint32 -> int32 by XOR with 0x80000000 to make signed comparison
+    work; pack int32 in the high 32 bits of int64 and the index in the low
+    32 bits. int64 signed descending order == float descending order.
+    """
+    bits = v.to(tl.uint32, bitcast=True)
+    sign = bits & 0x80000000
+    key = tl.where(sign != 0, ~bits, bits | 0x80000000)
+    m = (key ^ 0x80000000).to(tl.int32)
+    m_ext = m.to(tl.int64)
+    return (m_ext << 32) | i.to(tl.int64)
+
+
+@triton.jit
+def _unpack(packed):
+    """Unpack int64 into (float value, int64 index)."""
+    idx = (packed & 0xFFFFFFFF).to(tl.int64)
+    m_back = (packed >> 32).to(tl.int32)
+    key = (m_back ^ 0x80000000).to(tl.uint32)
+    sign_back = key & 0x80000000
+    bits = tl.where(sign_back != 0, key, ~key)
+    val = bits.to(tl.float32, bitcast=True)
+    return val, idx
+
+
+@triton.jit
+def topk_kernel(
+    x_ptr, val_ptr, idx_ptr,
+    N,
+    K: tl.constexpr,
+    BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr,
+    N_CHUNKS,
+):
+    """One CTA per row. Iteratively loads chunks, finds local top-K, and
+    merges with a running top-K buffer kept in registers."""
+    pid = tl.program_id(0)
+    base = pid * N
+
+    NEG_INF_F = float('-inf')
+    NEG_INF_I64: tl.constexpr = -(1 << 63)  # int64 min
+
+    running_packed = tl.full((K,), NEG_INF_I64, tl.int64)
+
+    for chunk_idx in range(N_CHUNKS):
+        chunk_start = chunk_idx * BLOCK_N
+        offs = chunk_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)
+        mask = offs < N
+        v = tl.load(x_ptr + base + offs, mask=mask, other=NEG_INF_F)
+        i = offs.to(tl.int64)
+
+        packed = _pack(v, i)
+        chunk_top = tl.topk(packed, K)
+
+        # Merge: cat [running, chunk_top] (size 2K) -> topk K
+        merged = tl.cat(running_packed, chunk_top)
+        running_packed = tl.topk(merged, K)
+
+    val, idx = _unpack(running_packed)
+    k_offs = tl.arange(0, K)
+    tl.store(val_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, val)
+    tl.store(idx_ptr + pid * K + k_offs, idx)
+
+
+def next_pow2(x):
+    if x <= 1:
+        return 1
+    return 1 << (x - 1).bit_length()
+
+
+OP_TYPE = "topk"
+SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["fp32"]
+HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]
+
+
+class Model(nn.Module):
+    """Top-k over the last dim of a 2D tensor.
+
+    Input:
+        x: (batch, n) fp32
+    Output:
+        values:  (batch, k) fp32, sorted descending
+        indices: (batch, k) int64, into the last dim of x
+    """
+
+    def __init__(self, batch: int, n: int, k: int):
+        super().__init__()
+        self.batch, self.n, self.k = batch, n, k
+        self.register_buffer("_dummy", torch.zeros(1))
+
+    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor):
+        B, N = x.shape
+        K = self.k
+
+        MAX_BLOCK_N = 16384
+        BLOCK_N = min(MAX_BLOCK_N, next_pow2(N))
+        N_CHUNKS = (N + BLOCK_N - 1) // BLOCK_N
+
+        val = torch.empty(B, K, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device)
+        idx = torch.empty(B, K, dtype=torch.int64, device=x.device)
+
+        if BLOCK_N <= 2048:
+            num_warps = 4
+        elif BLOCK_N <= 8192:
+            num_warps = 8
+        else:
+            num_warps = 16
+
+        grid = (B,)
+        topk_kernel[grid](
+            x, val, idx,
+            N, K, BLOCK_N, N_CHUNKS,
+            num_warps=num_warps,
+        )
+
+        return val, idx
+
+
+# Module-level shims rebuilt by check.py / benchmark.py per shape.
+batch = 64
+n = 8192
+k = 8
+
+
+def get_inputs():
+    x = torch.randn(batch, n, dtype=torch.float32)
+    return [x]
+
+
+def get_init_inputs():
+    return [batch, n, k]
args

1784	@builtin
1785	def cat(input, other, can_reorder=False, _semantic=None):
1786	    """
1787	    Concatenate the given blocks
1788	
1789	    :param input: The first input tensor.
1790	    :type input: Tensor
1791	    :param other: The second input tensor.
1792	    :type other: Tensor
1793	    :param reorder: Compiler hint. If true, the compiler is

+"""Quick test of solution on a single shape."""
+import torch
+import importlib.util
+spec = importlib.util.spec_from_file_location("solution", "/workspace/problems/05_topk_bitonic/solution.py")
+sol_mod = importlib.util.module_from_spec(spec)
+spec.loader.exec_module(sol_mod)
+
+import reference
+
+for shape in [{"batch": 64, "n": 8192, "k": 8}, {"batch": 32, "n": 16384, "k": 32}]:
+    reference.batch = shape["batch"]
+    reference.n = shape["n"]
+    reference.k = shape["k"]
+
+    init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
+    device = torch.device("cuda:0")
+    ref_model = reference.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
+    sol_model = sol_mod.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
+    sol_model.load_state_dict(ref_model.state_dict(), strict=True)
+
+    torch.manual_seed(42)
+    inputs = [t.to(device) for t in reference.get_inputs()]
+
+    with torch.no_grad():
+        ref_v, ref_i = ref_model(*inputs)
+        sol_v, sol_i = sol_model(*inputs)
+
+    # Compare values
+    val_close = torch.allclose(ref_v, sol_v, atol=1e-4, rtol=1e-4)
+    # Compare indices via gather
+    gathered = torch.gather(inputs[0], dim=-1, index=sol_i)
+    idx_close = torch.allclose(ref_v, gathered, atol=1e-4, rtol=1e-4)
+    print(f"Shape {shape}: values_close={val_close}, indices_gather_close={idx_close}")
+    if not val_close:
+        print(f"  ref_v[0,:5] = {ref_v[0,:5]}")
+        print(f"  sol_v[0,:5] = {sol_v[0,:5]}")
+    if not idx_close:
+        print(f"  ref_v[0,:5] = {ref_v[0,:5]}")
+        print(f"  gathered[0,:5] = {gathered[0,:5]}")
+        print(f"  sol_i[0,:5] = {sol_i[0,:5]}")
+        print(f"  ref_i[0,:5] = {ref_i[0,:5]}")

The issue is that the pack's XOR and unpack's XOR should cancel, but they don't because of the int32 vs uint32 interpretation. Let me think. The issue is that 0x80000000 is the same bit pattern as -2^31 in int32 and as 2^31 in uint32. XOR with 0x80000000 in int32 flips the sign bit. XOR with 0x80000000 in uint32 also flips bit 31 (the sign bit position). In pack: key (uint32) ^ 0x80000000. If Triton treats 0x80000000 as int32, it's -2147483648. XOR with -2147483648 in int32 is the same as XOR with 0x80000000 in uint32 (bit-flip the sign bit). So the result should be correct. The "key" is the monotonic encoding, not the original bits. So my encoding is: - For positive: key = bits | 0x80000000. To recover bits: bits = key & 0x7FFFFFFF (clear sign bit). Or: bits = key ^ 0x80000000 (XOR with sign bit). - For negative: key = ~bits. To recover bits: bits = ~key. In unpack, after getting key back: - If sign_back (bit 31) is set: original was positive, bits = key ^ 0x80000000 - If sign_back is not set: original was negative, bits = ~key Let me fix the unpack. ```python @triton.jit def _unpack(packed): idx = (packed & 0xFFFFFFFF).to(tl.int64) m_back = (packed >> 32).to(tl.int64) key = (m_back ^ 0x80000000).to(tl.uint32) sign_back = key & 0x80000000 # If sign_back set: original was positive, bits = key ^ 0x80000000 # If sign_back not set: original was negative, bits = ~key bits = tl.where(sign_back != 0, key ^ 0x80000000, ~key) val = bits.to(tl.float32, bitcast=True) return val, idx ``` Wait, but for positive floats, the sign bit of the original bits is 0. So bits & 0x80000000 = 0. We set the sign bit to 1 in key. So key & 0x80000000 = 0x80000000 (set). To recover bits from key (for positive): bits = key ^ 0x80000000 (XOR to flip the sign bit back to 0). This gives the original bits. For negative floats: original bits has sign bit 1. key = ~bits. So key has sign bit 0 (since ~ flips all bits). key & 0x80000000 = 0. To recover bits from key (for negative): bits = ~key (invert all bits back). This gives the original bits. So: - If sign_back set: bits = key ^ 0x80000000 - If sign_back not set: bits = ~key My current code has: - If sign_back set: bits = key (WRONG, should be key ^ 0x80000000) - If sign_back not set: bits = ~key (correct) Let me fix this. Wait, in my current code: ```python sign_back = key & 0x80000000 bits = tl.where(sign_back != 0, key, ~key) ``` This says: if sign_back is set (positive), bits = key. But key = bits | 0x80000000 for positive, so bits = key ^ 0x80000000, not key. Let me fix.
in 0out 0cache_r 0cache_w 0
assistant
I see the bug - the unpack is using the wrong inverse. Let me fix:

374	def _compare_and_swap(x, flip, i: core.constexpr):
375	    # compare-and-swap on the ith *innermost* dimension
376	    n_dims: core.constexpr = _log2(x.numel)
377	
378	    # flip along middle dimension (the bitwise XORs will be optimised away):
379	    idtype = _get_int_dtype(bitwidth=x.dtype.primitive_bitwidth, signed=True)
380	    ix = x.to(idtype, bitcast=True)
381	    iy = ix ^ xor_sum(ix, n_dims - 1 - i, True)
382	    y = iy.to(x.dtype, bitcast=True)
383	
384	    # determines whether we are in the right (rather than left) position along the axis:
385	    is_right = _indicator(n_dims, i)
386	
387	    # conditional swap:
388	    ret = core.where((x > y) != (flip ^ is_right), y, x)
389	    return ret

392	@jit
393	def _bitonic_merge_hypercube(x, stage: core.constexpr, order: core.constexpr):
394	    '''
395	    order_type 0 == ascending
396	    order_type 1 == descending
397	    order_type 2 == alternating
398	    '''
399	    # flip denotes whether to re-arrange sub-sequences of elements in ascending or
400	    # descending order.
401	    # if flip = 00000000... then all elements will be re-arranged ascendingly at this stage
402	    # if flip = 00110011... then all the elements will be re-arranged alternatingly (with
403	    # a stride of 2) at this stage
404	    if order == 2:
405	        flip = _indicator(_log2(x.numel), stage)
406	    else:
407	        flip = order
408	    # perform `stage` rounds of `compare-and-swap`

+"""Test if tl.sort works on int64."""
+import triton
+import triton.language as tl
+import torch
+
+
+@triton.jit
+def sort_test(x_ptr, out_ptr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr):
+    offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)
+    x = tl.load(x_ptr + offs)
+    sorted_x = tl.sort(x, 0, True)  # descending
+    tl.store(out_ptr + offs, sorted_x)
+
+
+def main():
+    x = torch.tensor([5, 2, 8, 1, 9, 3, 7, 4], dtype=torch.int64, device='cuda')
+    out = torch.empty(8, dtype=torch.int64, device='cuda')
+    sort_test[(1,)](x, out, BLOCK_N=8, num_warps=1)
+    print('input:', x)
+    print('sorted:', out)
+    # Expected: 9, 8, 7, 5, 4, 3, 2, 1
+
+
+if __name__ == '__main__':
+    main()
